Preface
This Master’s Thesis report of Mikkel Lunde Andersson was conducted between the 24th
of August to the 8th of February. The thesis is accountable for 32.5 ETCS points and is
conducted at the Department of Transport of the Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The supervisors of the conducted report have been Assistant Professor Dario Pacino and
Associate Professor Allan Larsen.
For fully understand this thesis it is assumed that the reader have a basic knowledge
of Operation Research.
As it is mentioned throughout the report, the mathematical model implemented in IBM
OLOG CPLEX Optimization Studio has been provided by Dario Pacino as well as the
used dataset.

==PAGE:4==
IV
PREFACE
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis considers the Container Stowage Planning Problem (CSP) of a container ves-
sel. The CSP attempts to minimize unproductive container movement on container ves-
sels. The vessels visit a number of ports, where containers needs to be loaded and un-
loaded onto the vessel. Containers can only be accessed from the top of each stack, which
may cause to unproductive container movement.
The aim of the thesis is to investigate the options of achieving feasible solutions with
the placement heuristic, while considering the stack weight constraint with a reasonable
amount of shifts. The performance of the developed heuristic approach in Dong and
Chou (2015) is tested, and compared to the performance of the mathematical model from
Avriel et al. (1998).
The thesis evaluates the possibility of improving the developed heuristic by implement-
ing a local search with a swap neighbourhood and still generates feasible stowage plans
with a reasonable amount of shift and a low computational time. Additionally, a 3-step
algorithm is implemented in order to obtain feasible stowage plans considering the stack
weight constraint using the heuristic approach in Ding and Chou (2015) as construction
heuristic.
Computational results show, that a local search with a swap neighbourhood does not
improve the stowage plan for in most instances. Furthermore, the results show that the
implementation of the stack weight constraint has a big inﬂuence on the average solu-
tion quality obtained with 3-step algorithm. A sensitivity analysis shows that the 3-step
algorithm is able to generate stowage plan with a reasonable amount of shifts if the stack
weight limit is slightly increased.
The average solution quality of the placement heuristic is improved by the local search.
The improvements are often small and not considerable relative to the number of loaded
containers and there is a need of further work for this extension. The performance of the
3-step algorithm is as good as the original placement heuristic, when there is just a slight
difference between the total container weight and the vessel weight capacity.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Today, containerization is one of the most important businesses within the transportation
sector. According to the World Shipping Council, 52% of the world’s seaborne trade is
transported in containers. The annual throughput worldwide for container ports was as
high as 651 million Twenty-foot Equivalent Unit (TEU) in 2013 and has grown by an aver-
age of 5.22% over the last ﬁve years. It is expected, that this tendency is to be continued
in the near future (UNCTAD, 2014).
The increasing demand for moving goods in containers from one place to another, has
resulted in an increased volume and ﬂeet size of the container vessels. The very ﬁrst
container vessel was set to sea in 1957 with a capacity of less than 100 TEU. Today’s
largest container vessels have a volume capacity of 19,224 TEU (MSC Oscar, MSC Oliver
and MSC Zoe). Furthermore, it is not only the volume of the largest vessels that has in-
creased. The average vessel capacity has almost doubled from 2,259 TEU in 2004, to 4,449
TEU in 2014. The total ﬂeet size is estimated to be over 6,000 vessels (UNCTAD, 2014).
This increasing demand has led to a higher level of competition and market shares be-
tween the various container terminals. Due to the ﬁerce competition, and limited ex-
pansion possibilities for some container ports, it is crucial for these container ports to be
as efﬁcient as possible in managing the containers. This has led to an increased focus
on optimizing processes within the container terminals. One of these problems is the ar-
rangement of containers on the vessels, which is known as the Container Stowage Planning
(CSP).
Generally speaking, an efﬁcient stowage plan minimizes the number of container move-
ments, since container movements are both time-consuming and expensive. In order to
obtain this goal, it is interesting to look at the unproductive container movements known
as shifts. Shifts occur when a container is unloaded and loaded in a port which is not
the container’s ﬁnal destination. Since containers can only be accessed from the top of a
stack, shifts occur when a container needs be unloaded in the current port but is located
1
==PAGE:18==
2
INTRODUCTION
beneath one or several containers with a destination for a later port. In order to access
the speciﬁc container, all containers stacked above must be shifted.
The CSP has been a research topic for operations researchers since the 1970’s. In the last
decade this research has intensiﬁed, and has been proved to be textitNP-Complete (NP,
Non-deterministic Polynomial Time) in Avriel et al. (2000). The aforementioned volume
increase of the overall ﬂeet size and the maximum vessel capacity, can be seen as two
factors that has drawn the attention to the development of high quality stowage plans.
Several solutions has been attempted, but the approaches that has been most successful
so far are decomposing the CSP into two smaller sub-problems. The ﬁrst problem assigns
containers into locations, which handles high-level constraints such as stability. The sec-
ond sub-problem deals with the slot assignment within each of the locations that handles
the stacking constraints. Pacino (2012) and Delgado (2013) have recently done studies
in collaboration with industrial companies, where the proposed research has been tested
on real-life industrial scenarios. Generally speaking, the access to conﬁdential industrial
data such as vessel-characteristics, demands, and stowage plan knowledge poses a chal-
lenge for researchers. As a result, many authors have been forced to test their research on
either ﬁctional (non-empirical), as in Ding and Chou (2015), where the instances are ran-
domly generated- or small real-life instances, as in Sciomachen and Tanfani (2003), where
the solution approach is tested on two industrial instances with a 1,800 TEU vessel.
1.1
Motivation And Thesis Question
The focus in the recently published studies has been on formulations applicable for use
in real life instances as in Pacino (2012) and Delgado (2013), where studies were done in
collaboration with industrial companies, instead of focussing on the core optimization
point of view of the CSP. These studies have shifted focus to master planning, where
containers are stowed into locations instead of speciﬁc slots (as in slot planning). Due
to the research made from the master planning perspective, it is now possible to obtain
optimal solutions to master planning. In Pacino (2012), the ﬁrst optimal master plans
where presented on a large benchmark set of industrial instances. This thesis aims to
research and answering the question:
”Is it possible to obtain better solution than the simple placement heuristic algorithm
presented in Ding and Chou (2015) with a local search with a swap neighbourhood.
Additionally is it possible to extend the algorithm to consider the stack weight limit
constraint and still obtain good feasible solutions within a time limit of 10 minutes?”
The heuristic placement approach presented in Ding and Chou (2015) is the ﬁrst real
pure placement heuristic developed since the one presented by Avriel et al. (1998) with a
few exceptions presented by Yoke et al. (2009) and Fan et al. (2010). However, these two
exceptions use a block design with locations to solve the CSP. This means that the studies
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 1
3
presented by Ding and Chou (2015) was the ﬁrst to deal with the objective to optimize
the core problem of CSP, and by doing so considering a minimum of constraints and fo-
cussing more on the objective than implementing as many constraints as possible in 15
years.
To the author’s knowledge, Ding and Chou (2015) is the only recent proposed article
with the purpose of optimizing the CSP without focussing on real-life scenarios. This
problem is a CSP without considerations of any constraints other than the most neces-
sary. This reduces the CSP to a simple state, which allows the focus to be exclusively on
the objective value. At this state, the CSP only considers the constraints that secures the
containers are not loaded into the same slot, while those containers loaded must have
support from below. Furthermore must the capacity of each stack not be violated. This
thesis will focus on the optimization of the pure CSP, in which the containers are assigned
to speciﬁc slots in a single bay. The subject of this thesis will be theoretically based on
the previous studies, presented mainly by Ding and Chou (2015), but will also use some
aspects from Avriel et al. (1998).
1.2
Report Structure
Chapter 2 Container Industry. This chapter will introduce an overview of the container
industry, as well as a brief description of the different types of containers and container
terminals. Finally, the chapter will give a more thorough description of container vessels
and the necessity of a good arrangement of containers, in order to comply the rules of
vessel stability and stress values. The terminology presented to the reader in this chapter
will be used in the rest of the thesis and should help the reader to fully understand the
Container Stowage Problem.
Similar chapters in Pacino (2012) and Delgado (2013) have been an inspiration for the
design of this chapter.
Chapter 3 Container Stowage Planning. This chapter describes the Container Stowage
Planning and how a stowage plan is characterised. Furthermore, the chapter describes
some of the most common objectives for Container Stowage Planning as well as some of
the stacking rules associated with arranging containers on a vessel. Finally. a mathemat-
ical model for the Container Stowage Planning is presented and described.
Chapter 4 Literature Review. This chapter contains a review and an overview of the
most relevant studies relating to the Container Stowage Problem.
Chapter 5 Solution Method. This chapter gives a description of the implemented al-
gorithm from Ding and Chou (2015) as well as the proposed local search method. Fur-
==PAGE:20==
4
INTRODUCTION
thermore, the proposed extension of the mathematical formulation is presented and de-
scribed together with the heuristic approach used for obtaining feasible solution and sat-
isfy the extended mathematical formulation. The presented solution approach is care-
fully described and pseudo code is presented. Additionally, a description of how the
data is generated as well as an analysis of the data layout used in this thesis is conducted.
This chapter also gives a description of the limitations and assumptions made for the
speciﬁc problem for this thesis.
Chapter 6 Performance Analysis. This chapter presents and analyses the results obtained
with the Main algorithm from Ding and Chou (2015), the implemented local search (LS)
algorithm with swap neighbourhood and for the 3-step algorithm, which solves the prob-
lem considering the stack weight constraint.
Chapter 7 Sensitivity Analysis This chapter presents a sensitivity analysis on the docu-
mented results with the 3-step algorithm.
Chapter 8 Conclusion. This chapter summarises the thesis, and concludes with ﬁnal
remarks. Finally, I propose the outline of possible future work
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
Container Industry
This chapter will give a theoretical description of seaborne shipping and the container
industry, which would be used in this thesis. Furthermore with an introduction to the
terminology that will be used later on in the thesis.
The ﬁrst section will give an introduction of the container industry itself, where the evo-
lution and current state of the industry will be described. The following section will give
an introduction to the most used type of containers there exist on the market. The section
after will give a description of the most common layout of a container terminal along
with a description of the equipment used in the terminal. The last section in this chap-
ter will introduce the different types and sizes of container vessels followed by a more
detailed description to the stability and stress limits of a container vessel.
2.1
Seaborne Shipping
Seaborne shipping has played a big role to the increasing globalization, where it is now
economically feasible to get goods transported from the other side of the world. This
require low transportation costs in order to be economically feasible . The low costs have
created an increasing demand for seaborne cargo transportation around the world. This
has contributed to the fact that seaborne shipping today is the most used shipping mode.
Seaborne shipping can be divided into three general shipping modes; tramp-, liner- and
industrial shipping.
Tramp shipping is a sort of taxa service where there does not exists any standard routes,
it is in other words needs-driven where cargo is transported directly from one destination
to another. It is typically large volumes of bulk cargoes with a low value for example raw
materials such as fertilizers, grain, coal, bauxite, fuel oil, aviation fuel and diesel, that are
transported with trampers.
Liner shipping refers to the transportation of several types of cargo in smaller volumes
than in tramp shipping. The transported cargo is typically packed in containers or pallets,
where it is easy to divide from each other since the cargo have different origins and des-
tinations. Furthermore liner shipping vessels are scheduled to visit a ﬁxed set of ports,
5
==PAGE:22==
6
CONTAINER INDUSTRY
no matter if the vessel is fully loaded or not.
In industrial shipping both cargo and vessels are owned by same owner, who tries to
minimize the total transportation cost. Due to the fact that liner shipping is the preferred
transportation mode for containers, this thesis would be concentrated on liner shipping.
Figure 2.1: MSC Oscar at Algeciras port, Spain 1
Containerization is known as a technique to transport cargo in containers on specialized
container vessels. Containers are metallic boxes of standard dimensions and types, where
both the size and type depends on the cargo. Container vessels are ships designed to
carry a huge number of containers and are transporting most of the seagoing non-bulk
cargo today. The vessels are assigned to a ﬁxed cyclic route of ports, with the purpose of
both unloading containers on-board and loading additional containers onto the vessel.
The capacity of container vessels is measured in Twenty-foot Equivalent Unit (TEU), which
is the space a 20’ container occupies on the ship. The most used container sizes are 20’
and 40’ containers, where a 40’ container is equivalent to two 20’ containers or two-TEU
spaces. The size of the largest container ship has grown rapidly through the years and
today the largest vessel has a capacity of 19,224 TEU (MSC Oscar, MSC Oliver and MSC
Zoe) pictured in Figure 2.1.
2.2
Containers
A shipping container is a reusable steel box with standardized dimensions and is de-
signed to withstand shipment, storage on top of each other and handling by big cranes.
The standardized dimensions is deﬁned by The International Organization of Standardiza-
tion (ISO), where containers are typically 20’, 40’ or 45’ long, while it is usually 8’ wide
and either 8.6’ or 9.6’ high. A 9.6’ high container is called high cube container. Figure 2.2

-ship1.html, accessed February 2016

illustrates the most used container dimensions.
Figure 2.2: Most used container dimensions taken from Pacino (2012)
2.2.1
Container Types
The cargo transported in containers can vary a lot and therefore there exist a lot of differ-
ent types of containers specialized to transport different types of cargo in the best possible
way.
One type of containers is known as reefer containers, which is capable of adjusting the
temperature inside the container to be either frozen, chilled or warm. Reefer containers
are for example needed, when fruits, vegetables or food need to be transported. The nose
of the reefer containers have incorporated a refrigeration system in order to maintain
the temperature, through the air duct system. It is however needed that the container
is plugged to a power supply. This limit the stowage area for reefer containers to slots
where a power supply is available, which is usually located in the bottom of the vessel.
Another type of containers is insulated containers, which is also used for transporting
temperature sensitive cargo, just as the reefer containers. Unlike the reefer containers,
the insulated containers do not have any refrigeration systems build into the construc-
tion, which then give the containers a higher capacity than the reefers. The insulated
containers do instead have 60 mm. thicker walls than standard containers, in order to
minimize the inﬂuence of the temperature outside the container. This means that the in-
sulated containers do not need to have access to a power supply.
Ventilated containers are a standard container provided with ventilation openings in both
the top and bottom of the side rails. The openings do not allow rain or spray to enter into
the container but secures an air circulation within the container, which can be usable for
transporting some kinds of food. A ventilated container does not have the need of any
electricity supply.
==PAGE:24==
8
CONTAINER INDUSTRY
Containers used for transporting liquids are called tank containers. Tank containers are
designed as a tank inserted into a metallic frame with the size of a standard container.
This makes it possible to stack tank containers above or below other type’s containers. In
terms of safety issues a tank container must be at least 80% and no more than 95% full
to avoid dangerous surging and secure expansion space for the liquids. If the liquid is
temperature sensitive, it is possible to either equip the tank with insulation or heating
and it then requires having access to one of the power supplies on the vessel.
Bulk containers are specially designed to transport bulk cargo, such as grain, feedstuffs
or spices, but they can also be used to transport normal cargo. They are designed with
three loading hatches in the roof of the container and some also have two unloading
hatches in the doorways.
Out-Of-Gauge (OOG) containers are used to transport cargo with a special size, which
does not ﬁt into a standard container. There exist four types of OOG containers; hard
top-, open top containers, ﬂatracks and platforms. Hard top containers are equipped with a
removable steel roof, which makes it possible to load from the top instead of through the
doors. Furthermore the roof can be fastened to the side of the container in case of high
cargo. Open top containers do not have any steel roof but do instead have a tarpaulin,
which makes it possible to transport tall cargo. A ﬂatrack is mainly used to transport
either cargo with over-height or over-width, since a ﬂatrack does not have any roof or
side walls. The two end walls of a ﬂatrack are stable enough to allow several ﬂatracks to
be stacked on top of each other. Finally platforms do not have any roof or walls at all,
which hereby make it possible to transport all sorts of oversized cargo.
A container used for transporting hazardous cargo, falls under the category Interna-
tional Maritime Organization (IMO) containers and must satisfy a set of regulation due
to stowage of these.
2.3
The Container Terminal
A container terminal is a part of a harbour, which is specialized in the handling of con-
tainers. This facility handles the containers on their way from the origin to the ﬁnal
destination. Containers can either be; import-, export- or transshipment containers. Import
containers are containers arriving with a vessel and needs to be distributed by land trans-
port, such as trucks or trains in order to go to the local customers. Export containers are
the opposite of import containers and are coming from the local area and needs to be
transshipped to a vessel. Transshipment containers are on the other hand containers ar-
riving with a vessel and then needs to be stored at the terminal area until the vessel going
to the container destination or the next transshipment location berths the terminal.
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2
9
The container terminal can typically be divided into three sections; the quayside, the yard
and the landside, where a typical layout is illustrated in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Overview of classic container terminal layout taken from Pacino (2012)
The quayside of the terminal is the area located closest to the sea and is the place, where
the vessels arrive and are assigned a berth in order to unload and load containers. Each
unloading and loading operation of a container - either to or from the vessels is handled
by quay cranes and is called a move. The number of moves are used to determine the price
for the liner shipping companies and the crane utilization. Therefore it is essential to
keep the number of moves to a minimum, since it increases both the berthing time and
the price.
The yard is the area where containers are stored temporarily, while waiting for either a
land vehicle or a vessel to transport it further on the route. The containers are stored in
stacks at the yard, which are accessed by gantry cranes. In the yard there is special loca-
tions reserved for different types of containers for example reefers, hazardous or empty
containers.
The landside is the area farthest away from the sea and is the connection point to the local
area, where land vehicles either deliver or pick-up containers. In order to transport con-
tainers between the three sections or around the yard either passive or active vehicles can
move the containers. Active vehicles, such as forklifts, straddle carriers and reachstackers all
need a driver, while passive vehicles such as automated guided vehicles (AGV), trucks with
trailers or multi-trailers is more advanced and do not need any driver in order to operate.

CONTAINER INDUSTRY
2.4
Container Vessels
Container vessels are specially designed ships to transport containerized cargo over the
sea. The ﬁrst container ship ever build was in 1960 with a capacity of 610 TEU. Due to
higher demand, improved globalization and higher competition in the shipping industry
the need of bigger vessels has existed from the beginning of container transportation. The
evolution of containers ship capacities can be observed in Figure 2.4, where todays largest
container ship have a capacity of 19,224 TEU.
Figure 2.4: Evolution of largest container vessels measured in TEU capacity
2.4.1
Container Vessel Types
It is not beneﬁcial to transport all containers on very big vessels with a high capacity,
since those ships are very expensive to operate. The demand for some ports or routes are
not that high and therefore are also smaller vessels needed, which are cheaper to operate.
Container vessels do most often serve a ﬁxed route, whereas the size and capacity of
ship depends on the demand for the related route. According to the different sizes and
capacity of the container vessels, it is possible to divide them into several types according
to (MAN Diesel and Turbo, 2008).
• Small Feeders are the smallest vessels with a capacity of less than 1,000 TEU, they
are often used for very short distance transport of containers.
• Feeders have a capacity of 1,000 - 2,799 TEU and are used to supply bigger vessels
with containers from smaller ports, which do not have a high enough container
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2
11
demand to supply the larger vessels.
• Panamax vessels are named after the Panama Canal, since those ships are the biggest
ships that can sail through the Panama Canal. Those vessels have a capacity rang-
ing from 2,800 - 5,099 TEU.
• Post Panamax vessels are named after the fact, that they were the ﬁrst vessels ex-
ceeding the size of the Panama Canal, they do have a capacity ranging from 5,500 -
9,999 TEU.
• New Panamax ships are the ones that do ﬁt to the new Panama Canal, which is
currently under construction and will open in 2016. The new canal will be wider
and hereby the vessels are also bigger in both size and capacity and ranging from
12,000 - 14,499 TEU.2
• Ultra Large Container Vessels (ULCV) are the last type of vessels with a capacity ex-
ceeding 14,500 TEU does this type include the biggest vessels in the existing ﬂeet.
The size of ULCV does already exceed the size of the new Panama Canal. These
vessels are used to transportation of cargo over long distances and from some of
the biggest ports. The high capacity makes it possible to lower the marginal cost of
transporting one container over a long distance.
The worldwide cellular vessel ﬂeet size is according to Alphaliner (2015) on 5,111 ships
out of a total of 6,032 ships, where the cellular ﬂeet capacity is 19,369,705 TEU. It can be

active accounting for 37.35% of the vessels distribution. It is on the other Post Panamax,
which is accounting for the largest capacity of TEU with 35.99% of the total cellular ﬂeet
capacity.

2.4.2
Vessel Structure and Layout
Containers are arranged in the container vessels due to the bay-stack-tier system, which is
a numerical system of positions at the vessels according to the structure of the vessel.
Bays are the numbers along the length of the vessel ascending from the front also known
as the bow to the back or stern of the vessel. 20’ containers are stowed in odd bays, while
either 40’ or 45’ containers are stowed in even bays. Due to this structure, an even bay
consists of two contiguous odd bays numbered one below and above the even number
bay. Vertically the vessel is divided into two sections below-deck or on-deck where these
two sections are divided by a hatch-cover. Below-deck containers are stowed in a cargo
hold, while those containers stowed on-deck are stowed on top of the hatch-cover. If
containers need to be loaded below-deck, all containers standing on the top of the hatch-
cover must be removed before the hatch-cover can be lifted off and containers can be
loaded. In Figure 2.5 a longitudinal view of a container vessel is presented, where the
above-mentioned factors can be observed.
Figure 2.5: A longitudinal view of a container vessel taken from Pacino (2012)
Each bay consists of vertical piles of containers stowed on top of each other known as
stacks. Stacks are numbered with odd numbers from the left of the center, while even
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 2
13
numbers are to the right of the center in ascending order. If there are an odd number
of stacks within the bay, the numbering of the center stack starts by 00 illustrated in
Figure 2.6.
Figure 2.6: Stack numbering on a container vessel with either even and odd number of stacks.
A stack consists of two vertical piles of containers, where each vertical position in the
stack is known as a cell and has a capacity of 2 TEU and can either contain two 20’ or one
40’ container. A cell can be split into two slots, which has a capacity of 1 TEU limited to
contain only 20’ containers. The slot closest to the bow of the cell is called the fore slot,
while the aft slot is located towards the stern. Some slots are design with extra features
for containers with special demands presented in Section 2.2. This could for example be
a slot with the possibility of assigning a power supply to the container in that slot.
Figure 2.7: Inner- and outer sockets taken from Delgado (2013)
Each stack has some limitations both due to the height and the maximum weight. As a
stack consists of two contiguous slots, there do exist both a maximum weight limit for
the inner socket used by 20’ containers and for the outer socket used by both 20’ and 40’
containers as it is shown in Figure 2.7. If the stack is a russian stacks, where it is a mixture
of both 20’ and 40’ containers, the weight of 20’ containers are divided evenly between
the inner- and outer socket. Usually the weight limit is smaller for the inner socket than
the outer sockets, since the vessel is weaker in the middle. The maximum height of the
==PAGE:30==
14
CONTAINER INDUSTRY
stacks are either limited by the hatch cover or by the line-of-sight, which ensures that the
captain can see the sea surface from the bridge at least two ship lengths or 500 m away.
The vertical index of the bay is known as tiers. The tiers is numbered by even num-
bers starting at the bottom of the cargo hold with 02, while the index on-deck starts at 82
and is incremented by two for each vertical position.
To secure the stability of the vessel, the stacks are grouped into different storage areas
called locations. The storage areas are divided into containing containers either below-
deck or on-deck. The grouped stacks do not necessarily need to be consecutive, since
stacks towards the port and seaside are merged together in the same location. The loca-
tions do not exist physically on the ship, but is only used for stowage planning in order
to approximated stability calculations. Figure 2.8 shows a possible division of location
for a single bay.
Figure 2.8: Stacks divided into locations taken from Delgado (2013)
Ballast tanks are tanks located around the vessel, which makes it possible to modify the
stability conditions by adjusting the water level within the tanks. The ballast tanks on
the sides are often used, when loading and unloading containers in the ports and act as
heeling.
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 2
15
2.4.3
Vessel Stability
When container vessels are set to leave a port, it must be declared seaworthy, which
means it must satisfy all stability- and stress values must be within certain limits. In
order to obtain both an acceptable stress limits and stability values all items (e.g. contain-
ers, ballast water, fuel etc.) loaded at the vessel must be taken into consideration.
The total weight of a container vessel is known as the displacement and can be determined
by the portion of the hull under the waterline. The plane where the waterline cuts the
hull is called the water plane. When the vessel is loaded at full capacity or maximum dis-
placement, the hull will meet the water at the designed waterline. The vertical line passing
through the most forward part of the vessel at the designed water line is known as the
Fore Perpendicular (FP). The vertical line opposite the FP, passing through the most back
part of the vessel at the designed waterline is called the Aft Perpendicular (AP). Midship
is the midpoint between the AP and FP. The bottom of the vessel is known as the keel
and the draft is the distance between the keel and the waterline.
If the weight distribution is totally even, the waterline and the keel would be parallel and
the vessel is said to have an even keel. Even though the vessel does not have an even keel,
it is still possible for the vessel to be seaworthy by satisfying certain criteria. The fore draft
is the draft at the FP and the aft draft is the draft at the AP. The draft values do have
to satisfy three simple rules, ﬁrst of all the aft draft has to be so high that the propeller
is covered by water for the vessel to actually sail. Next the maximum aft- or fore draft
must be smaller than the allowed limit when arriving at a port, so the keel is not hitting
the bottom. Finally for the cranes to work properly, the maximum draft must be above a
certain limit.
The trim is measured as the difference between the aft- and fore draft, where a positive
trim value means that the vessel is trimming towards the stern and for a negative trim
value the vessel is trimming towards the bow. The angle between the waterline and the
keel is the trim angle (δ), W1 is the waterline parallel to the keel and W2 is the actual wa-
terline and the trim angle is hereby the angle between W1 and W2. Figure 2.9 illustrates
the trim and draft for different waterlines.
Figure 2.9: The draft, trim and waterlines from a longitudinal view taken from Pacino (2012)
==PAGE:32==
16
CONTAINER INDUSTRY
The weight of the vessel acts down through the center of gravity (G), which is the point
where the gravitational force acts and is resisted by upward buoyancy force equal to the
weight. The buoyant force is acting through the center of buoyancy (B), which is the center
of gravity of the volume of water displaced by the vessel. In Figure 2.10 the center of
gravity G and the center of buoyancy B1 shows the initial state of a stable vessel where
the center of gravity is vertically aligned with the center of buoyancy. If an external force,
such as wind or waves, causes the vessel to heel to one side, the center of gravity G will
not move, while the center of buoyancy will move to B2. The intersection between the
center line going through the gravity center G and the new center of buoyancy B2 is the
metacenter (M), which is the point where all vertical forces is said to act. The distance
from the center of gravity G to the metacenter M is called the metacentric height (GM), a
big metacentric height GM means that the vessel is more stable. The metacentric height
may not be too big, since it can cause the vessel to be stiff. A high stiffness will resist
the vessel from rolling from side to side but if the vessel starts rolling it will obtain a
high speed, which could be dangerous for both the crew and loaded cargo. In order for a
vessel to be declared stable the metacentric height must be above a certain value.
Figure 2.10: The stability component from a transversal view taken from Pacino (2012)
It can be assumed for small heeling angles (θ) that the volume of wedges will grow linear
with θ. When the vessel is no longer exposed for an external force an equal opposite force,
will bring back the vessel to the original position. The righting lever (Z) is the projection
of G on the B2 · M and can be calculated to be Mr = δ · Z = δ · GM · sin(θ) and is the
moment for the vessel to get back to the original position.
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2
17
2.4.4
Vessel Stress Limits
The stress factors of the vessel are also important factors due to the safety issues and
must be kept within limits. In order to keep the structural stress within the limits, it is
necessary to look at how a vessel is inﬂuenced by vertical forces. A ship can be divided
into several ﬁctional longitudinal sections along the vessel to estimate the stress of the
structure.
Each longitudinal section of the vessel does have different weight, which depend on e.g.
how many and what type of containers loaded in the section. Further the weight of a
section does also depend on the structural design of the vessel for that section. As it can
be seen in Figure 2.11 some sections are marked with a downward arrow, which shows
that the weight of the section is higher than the buoyancy force. A heavy section will
press the vessel further down into the water until displaced water will equal the weight
of the section. Other sections are instead marked with an upward arrow meaning that
the weight is less than the buoyancy force. This will cause the vessel to raise up until the
displaced water equals the section weight. The different effects of both buoyance force
and weight for the longitudinal sections cause the vessel structure to create stress forces
for the vessel structure. For safety issues the stress forces of the vessel may be within
some limits, which are given as points along the vessel, these limits are known as frames.
Figure 2.11: The stress forces from a longitudinal view taken from Delgado (2013)
The shear force of a frame can be approximated to be the total exposure of vertical forces
around the frame, hereby as the sum of vertical forces for frames aft and fore the current
frame. One of the vertical forces is the downward force from the weight loaded in the
section. The weight can be calculated by adding the weight of the total load within the
section combined with the structural weight of the vessel in the section. Another vertical
force is the upward buoyancy force, which is not as straightforward to calculate as the
weight. The buoyancy force depends on the weight of the water displaced by the hull
for that section. The water density is known and the volume of water displaced by the
hull can be estimated from the shape of the hull. Often the hull takes an irregular shape,
so several reference points exists along the hull called stations. The immersed area of the
transverse section of the hull can be estimated with a function of draft by the bonjean curve
==PAGE:34==
18
CONTAINER INDUSTRY
of the stations. Thereby the volume of water displaced by the section of the hull can be
calculated as the immersed area between all sets of contiguous stations. The immersed
volume is for two consecutive stations, the distance between the two stations multiplied
with the average area at the current draft, which can be used to estimate the buoyancy
force of the section.
Another stress factor is the bending moment, which needs to be within certain limits. The
bending moment is the reaction to the vessel done by the vertical forces, which cause the
vessel to bend. It is measured as how much the vessel is bending around a certain point
and calculated by multiplying each vertical force for the section aft and fore the frame
with the distance to the current frame to where the forces acts.
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3
Container Stowage Planning
This chapter will give a detailed description of the Container Stowage Planning (CSP). The
chapter will furthermore introduce some of the speciﬁed stacking rules associated with
container vessels. Container vessels do have to comply a set of stowage rules in order to
be declared seaworthy. These rules are explained in Chapter 2 alongside with the speciﬁc
rules for different kinds of containers introduced in Section 2.2.
First of all the human planners do have to consider the objectives associated with con-
tainer stowage, further described in Section 3.2. This should be done with respect to the
safety of both the vessel and the cargo on board. Consequently there are several rules for
a container ship to be declared seaworthy, which are described in Section 2.4.
3.1
Stowage Plan
A stowage plan is a representation of a loading plan for a container vessel. It shows exactly
in which slot each container should be placed. The stowage plan is preformed by human
planners before the vessel calls the next port on it’s route. It must specify exactly which
containers that needs to be unloaded and furthermore, which containers to be loaded
onto the vessel. The planners are provided with a loading list of containers, a release list of
containers and information about both the ship and port design. They do usually have a
time window of approximately six hours to preform the stowage plan.
The loading list is a list of all containers, which needs to be loaded onto the vessel from
the port yard. It contains detailed information about the containers such as dimension,
type, weight and destination. The release list is a similar list of containers, which needs to
be unloaded from the container vessel and to the yard. It contains the same information
as the loading list, along with the exact position the container is stowed in. A vessel proﬁle
is information about a ship and is provided to the planner. The vessel proﬁle contains
information such as a status of the ship’ capacity, size, position of reefer slots, fuel, diesel,
oil and ballast water etc., while the port information states how many quay cranes there
are available, the port depth and available berths.
19
==PAGE:36==
20
CONTAINER STOWAGE PLANNING
Stowage plans are created for each of the ports visited by the container vessel. It is also
necessary for the planners to consider the stowage plans, not only for the current port
but also for subsequent ports. This is done by forecasting the demand for the subsequent
ports based on historical data. These forecasts are not always accurate and consequently
there exists a general set of rules, which are met if possible. Generally are containers
with the same discharge port loaded in as few locations as possible. It is furthermore
tried to keep the number of different discharge ports within each location at a minimum.
Reefer slots are reserved for reefer container and the planners avoid stowing non-reefers
in those slots if possible.
3.2
Container Stowage Objectives
There exist lots of different objectives for container stowage. The companies within the
liner shipping have the main objective of making money. This is equivalent with maxi-
mization of the proﬁt and is one of the most important objective’ for the industrial com-
panies in order to survive. One way to increase the proﬁt is to maximize the intake on
each container vessel, which enlarges the number of containers on-board the container
vessel and the total income. Furthermore a higher intake will lower the marginal cost of
transporting one container and gives the company a competitive advantage, since they
can either lower the price or increase the proﬁt.
Maximization of income is not the only key objective in container stowage. It is also im-
portant to lower the costs associated with the transportation. One of the key objectives
for lowering the costs are to minimize the port stay also known as the berthing time, since
it is very costly for a vessel to stay in the port. In order to do so, the stowage plan must
minimize the overstowage and hereby the number of redundant container moves. A re-
dundant container move exists, when a container is temporary unloaded for then to be
loaded again to the same container vessel. It is also necessary to maximize the utilization
of the quay cranes, having as many quay cranes to work simultaneously as possible.
Another objective is to minimize the ballast water, since the use of ballast water either
increases the fuel consumption or lowers the vessel speed. As well as it is important for
the companies to make money, there is today an increased focus on green transportation.
It is hereby important to lower the emission and air pollution.
In almost all approaches to the CSP, the objective is to minimize the cost by either min-
imizing the berthing time or the overstowage. In those cases, the revenue is seen as a
constant and it is therefore not included in the formulation of the problem.
In this thesis the objective is to minimize the number of overstowage containers.
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 3
21
Overstowage
Overstowage is if a container destined to the current port is stowed in the middle of a
stack of containers with discharge port for a later port. It is then necessary to temporar-
ily unload all containers above the one destined to the current port, these containers are
said to be overstowaging containers. Hatch cover overstowage is when a container in
the hold has to be unloaded in the current port, while one or several containers above
the hatch cover are destined for ports later on the route. In this case all containers above
the hatch cover are overstowage containers, since they need to be temporarily unloaded
in order to gain access to the container destined for the current port. If a stack contains
one or more overstowing containers, it is said to be out of order, while a stack without any
overstowing containers are said to be in order.
Redundant moves of overstowing containers are called shifts and occurs when a con-
tainer is temporarily removed. There exists two different kinds of shifts, necessary shifts
(NS) and voluntary shifts VS. NS are when the vessel berths a port and a container for that
port is overstowed by at least one container. These shifts are NS, since they are unavoid-
able. VS are on the other hand not forced shifts and they are made to prevent costlier
shifts at later ports. To avoid costlier shifts at later ports, it can be beneﬁcial to re-order a
stack before loading additionally containers onto the stack.
To illustrate an example of overstowage, consider two containers; container c and k with
discharge ports d(c) = i and d(k) = j, where i is an arbitrage port and j > i. When the
vessel calls port i, if container k is stowed above container c in the same stack, it is neces-
sary to temporary remove container k in order to gain access to container c. Afterwards
container k will have to be loaded onto the vessel again, so it can be transported to the
ﬁnal destination, j. In such scenario container k is said to be overstowaging container c
and the extra container movements caused by the overstowage is known as shifts.
3.3
Stacking Rules
There are several stacking rules on board a container vessel, which helps to secure the
safety of both containers, vessels and the staff . Stacking rules inﬂuence the safety of
both the vessel and the cargo, since badly stacked containers can be the reason of major
accidents. Hereby is it not possible to place containers freely on board the ship. Most
stacking rules apply for a single stack and can be extended to be valid for all container
stacks on the vessel.
Stowage Below Deck
The stowage area below deck is called a hold and is usually only applicable for 20’ or
40’ containers, since the stacks in the hold often is designed to be exactly 40’ long. It is
therefore not possible to stow 45’ containers below deck for most vessels. Each stack in
the hold is divided by cell guides, which are used as a guidance for loading containers
into the hold and help support the stacks. Further in between each tier, the containers
==PAGE:38==
22
CONTAINER STOWAGE PLANNING
are secured with stacking cones. The stacking cones are an important factor in order to
minimize the container movement during oversea transportation and they are placed on
the container corners before loading. Hereby there are one stacking cone attached to each
of the container’ corner ﬁttings, in order to stabilize each stack as much as possible. Most
companies have an internal stacking rule, which only allows 40’ containers in the top tier
below deck in order to secure the stack the most. It can hereby be necessary to create
russian stacks in order to maintain this stacking rule. The placement of ballast tanks and
other structural design criteria such as the shape of the hull, do limit some of the stacks
below deck to have a capacity of 1 Twenty-foot Equivalent Unit (TEU) instead of the normal
capacity of 2 TEU. This limit those stacks to containing only 20’ containers in those slots,
where the stack has a limited capacity before larger containers can be loaded onto the
stack, these slots are known as odd-slots. It is not only stacks with odd-slots, which are
limited to only contain 20’ containers, since some stacks can be equipped with cell guides
in the middle of the stack which limits the stack to only contain 20’ containers.
Stowage On Deck
For stowage on deck the same set of stacking rules as below deck must be satisﬁed. There
are several other rules, which must be complied due to the safety of both cargo and the
vessel. These extra safety rules exist, since containers on deck do not have any support
from the vessel structure. Above deck the stacking cones are replaced with twist locks,
which are also placed in the container’ corner ﬁttings. These can lock containers to each
other, when the cone heads are twisted. Today most twist locks are automatic, which
secure that a twist lock locks the cone head when it is twisted and automatically releases
when the container is lifted.
Besides the twist locks, the two ﬁrst tiers on deck are secured with lashing rods, while the
tiers above are only secured with twist locks. Some vessels are equipped with lashing
bridges, which are secured to the vessel structure. A lashing bridge only allows loading
of either 20’ or 40’ containers in the bottom two tiers. It is then possible to secure the next
two tiers with lashing rods, if the vessel has a lashing bridge. When the ship is equipped
with a lashing bridge, the stacks can be higher due to the extra stability of the stacks.
Since containers above the ones secured with lashing rods are only secured by twist locks
and hereby only to each other, another important factor is that the stacks do have support
from each other. Due to this may stacks located besides each other may not have a height
difference of more than one in order to secure the stack stability. Furthermore may the
height of a stack not violated the line of sight.
It is also important to consider the rolling moment for the stacks on deck. Especially for a
vessel with a large metacentric height (GM) that have a short rolling period, which would
expose the top tiers of the stacks on deck for a strong force. Heavy containers are not
allowed in the top tiers, since this would intensify the force. Due to this heavy containers
are stowed in the lower tiers, while the lighter containers are stowed in the top tiers in
order to avoid any stack collapses. All of these stacking rules and speciﬁcations of the
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 3
23
Figure 3.1: Container Securing.
layout for a container vessel is stated in the vessel proﬁle for the ship.
3.4
Mathematical Model In Avriel Et Al. (1998)
The mathematical model presented in Avriel et al. (1998) considers a container vessel of
a single bay for container stowage. The horizontal index of the bay is tiers t = {1, ..., T},
where the bottom tier is equivalent with t = 1. Vertically the bay is divided into stacks
ranging from s = {1, ..., S}, where s = 1 is the leftmost stack of the bay. It is assumed that
there are space for T containers within each stack and that each stack only consist of a sin-
gle line of containers. The bay for stowage is considered two-dimensional even though
a bay in practise is three-dimensional, since each stack is arranged as two-dimensional.
Such bay is known as a rectangular bay, with a capacity limit of P = S · T. It is further
assumed that all containers are of the same standard size and neither including special
requirements such as reefer- or hazardous container. It is assumed that the bay is empty,
when the container vessel berths the ﬁrst port on the route. The route of the vessel starts
in port 1 and is then sequentially visiting port 2, 3, ..., N. In each port i = {1, ..., N −1}
containers can be loaded with possible destination j = {i + 1, ..., N}. When the vessel
berths the last port N, it is assumed that all containers must be unloaded. Whenever
the vessel leaves port i, the container arrangement remains unchanged until the vessel
arrives at port i + 1. The transportation matrix τ = [Tij] is a (N −1) · (N −1) matrix and
contains information of containers, origin in port i and destination in port j and is as-
sumed to be known in advance. Tij is an upper triangular matrix, where Tij = 0 for all
i ≥j.
The mathematical formulation in Avriel et al. (1998) presents the CSP as a (0, 1) binary
formulation with the binary decision variables xijvst and yist, modelled in Equation 3.6
and Equation 3.7. If xijvst = 1, then a container in slot (s, t) is loaded in port i and un-
loaded in port v, but with a ﬁnal destination of j, otherwise xijvst = 0. If v = j, then
the container is unloaded at the ﬁnal destination. While if v ≤j, then the container is
shifted, since the unloading destination vary from the ﬁnal destination for the container.
==PAGE:40==
24
CONTAINER STOWAGE PLANNING
If yist = 1 then it is said that the slot (s, t) is occupied by a container, when the vessel is
leaving port i and yist = 0 otherwise.
The objective function Equation 3.1 of the mathematical formulation is to minimize the
the number of shifts made for the vessel along the route of N ports. There are not dis-
tinguished about the different slot positions around the vessel and the cost of shifting a
container is 1.

Constraint 3.2 ensures that all containers must be loaded on-board the vessel going from
port i to j. While Constraint 3.3 secures that there is not loaded more than one container
in the same slot (s, t) on the vessel at any time. In Constraint 3.4 it is formulated that
each container loaded in slot (s, t) must have support from below either in terms of the
vessel or from another container in the slot below. Finally Constraint 3.5 ensures that if a
container must be shifted in port v, then all containers loaded above slot (s, t) must also
be shifted.
The above presented mathematical model is of almost the exact same formulation as the
model presented in Ding and Chou(2015), see Appendix B. The proposed mathematical
model in this thesis is the one presented in this section.
