Chapter 4
Literature Review
The CSP has been widely studied since the 1970’s. In this chapter, a review over the
previous academic studies relating to this thesis will be presented. This chapter will
provide a detailed description of the literature directly associated with the same solution
approach as used in the thesis, which is presented in Chapter 5. Further literature relating
to other solution approaches, with and without decomposition, will be presented in this
chapter.
4.1
Container Stowage Planning
Avriel et al. (2000) proved the Container Stowage Planning (CSP) to be a Non-deterministic
Polynomial Time (NP)-Complete problem, which is strongly dependent on the ship capac-
ity due to the combinatorial high number of variables and constraints. Thus, it is not
possible or realistic to ﬁnd optimal solution if the examined instances are close to real
life scenarios. Even though some approaches aiming at optimality for the CSP, a lot of
approaches also try to use heuristics in order to locate a good quality solution, which is
far more relevant for the industry due to the time limitations. Overall the literature can
be divided into two different types of approaches, one, which attempts to solve the prob-
lem with a single model, and one, which decompose the problem into several multi-level
optimization models.
4.1.1
Placement Heuristics
Several authors have proposed different placement heuristics in order to solve the CSP,
where containers are placed onto the vessel given a set of predeﬁned rules. The advan-
tage of placement heuristic is that it is often possible to ﬁnd a good initial solution within
a reasonable amount of computational time. A pure placement heuristic needs to con-
sider all possible scenarios, when a set of containers needs to be placed on-board of a
vessel. In order to generate a stowage plan with a low number of shifts, it is often prob-
lematic to develop a solution that takes all scenarios into account. As a result, it is often
seen that pure placement heuristics do not consider all the constraints associated with the
27
==PAGE:44==
28
LITERATURE REVIEW
arrangement of containers on a vessel. If several constraints are considered in the place-
ment heuristic, it is not guaranteed that the heuristic would provide a feasible solution
and modiﬁcations to the initial solution could be necessary.
Avriel et al. (1993) presented a very simple placement heuristic, the Whole Column al-
gorithm, (WC-algorithm), which is combined with a 0-1 binary Linear Programming (LP)
formulation further described in Section 4.1.3.1. In the WC-algorithm, containers with
the same origin and destination are assigned to the same stack, which would eliminate
overstowage, since all containers in a stack is both loaded and unloaded in the same port.
At each port, the original transportation matrix is decomposed into two sub-matrices the
”whole columns”, which is a multiple of the number of tiers and the ”remainders”. In
Avriel et al. (1993) an example is presented for a ship with a single bay and the algorithm
obtains a solution without overstowage. The WC-algorithm is only applicable for rela-
tively small instances, since the number of ”remainding” containers might require a long
computational time to solve with the relatively simple mathematical model presented in
the study. The WC-algorithm is a solution approach, which has provided the foundation
for many of the placement heuristics developed afterwards.
The same authors present another placement heuristic called the Suspensory Heuristic Pro-
cedure, SH-algorithm in Avriel et al. (1998). The Myopic Shifting Rule gives the possibility
of making voluntary shifts in order to prevent costlier shifts in future ports. Further the
SH-algorithm is a pure placement heuristic in contrast to the WC-algorithm, where con-
tainers going to the farther ports are placed in the bottom and containers going to the
closer ports are ”hung” in the top of the stack. Like the WC-algorithm, the SH-algorithm
only considers a single bay ship without any consideration of constraints, such as weight
limitations, stability or different types of containers. The SH-algorithm does manage to
obtain substantial improvements compared to the WC-algorithm, where instances up to
a 1,700 TEU vessel visiting 30 ports are solved within 30 seconds. However, even though
the SH-algorithm shows huge improvements in terms of overstowage, the model does
not consider any stacking constraints which is crucial for real-life instances. The imple-
mentation of the Myopic Shifting Rule is a huge improvement for the placement heuristic,
since the use of voluntary shifts decreases the number of overstowage containers signiﬁ-
cantly for larger vessels.
Yoke et al. (2009) presented another pure placement heuristic with the objective to mini-
mize the overstowage and maximize the crane utilization. The heuristic divides the ship
into blocks, where a block contains all possible locations above or below the hatch cover.
Next, all containers are grouped by size, type and destination and lastly, containers are
stowed by groups into the ship blocks instead of one at the time. The ﬁrst placement
of the container groups does not consider the constraints for stability, hazardous segre-
gation and stack weight, where the last two are handle by following modules according
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 4
29
to the heuristic. The authors present a case study with a 5,000 TEU container ship for
a voyage of 8 ports, where containers are both unloaded and loaded in each port and
the heuristic manages to solve the case study within 1 minute. The heuristic can not
guarantee a feasible solution, since the stability of the container ship is never considered.
Fan et al. (2010) has extended the heuristic, so it can handle the stability constraints as
a swapping algorithm also was implemented in order to improve the original heuristic
approach.
In the article by Ding and Chou (2015) a simple placement heuristic is presented, where
the heuristic algorithm is based on a set of 10 rules in order to determine the placement
of each container. Further a method to determine whether or not voluntary unloading
movements would be able to save later necessary unloading movements are presented
as the Myopic Voluntary Shifting Determination (MVSD), see Section 5.2 for further descrip-
tion. The MVSD procedure is inspired by the deﬁnition of ”reorder up to” from Avriel
et al. (1998) and ”rearranged up to” in Aslidis (1984). The authors claim that the im-
plemented heuristic algorithm is more efﬁcient than the SH-algorithm. The results do
especially gets improved, when the number of ports visited or the ship capacity is large.
Due to the MVSD and the 10 rule procedure, the heuristic algorithm manages through
voluntary shifts to obtain a good solution quality, when only considering the total num-
ber of shifts. As the WC- and SH-algorithms, the heuristic algorithm is not applicable for
real-life scenarios, as it only considers the destination of each container and does not in-
clude any other constraints such as weight-, balance- or mixture constraints but only the
capacity constraints. As a result, this algorithm is not applicable for solving a full real-life
scenario, since it would lead to infeasible solutions due to the safety issues which must
be satisﬁed, described in Section 2.4 and Section 3.3. The Main algorithm in this article
claims to be very efﬁcient and further has some very promising results in terms of a low
number of overstowage containers.
The Main algorithm presented in Ding and Chou (2015) is described in details in Sec-
tion 5.2.
Even though both Avriel et al. (1998) and Ding and Chou (2015) both consider the pos-
sibility of voluntary shifting. There are no real possibility of changing any bad decision
made by the pre-designed set of rules, which can be troubled catching and optimizing
every single scenario considering both the stowage plan and the load-list. Both Yoke et
al. (2009) and Fan et al. (2010) consider both overstowage and crane intensity, where it
can be hard to improve both at the same time. Both do consider the possibility of ad-
justing the ﬁrst made assignment by several adjustment modules, though neither fully
describes how these adjustment modules operate.
==PAGE:46==
30
LITERATURE REVIEW
4.1.2
Placement Heuristics With Local Search
In order to improve the solutions based on a placement heuristic, some authors extend
the placement heuristic with a local search method to obtain better solutions. Scott and
Chen (1978) were the ﬁrst to combine a placement heuristic with a local search procedure.
The placement heuristic initially groups containers in 10 groups by type, size, weight,
ranking strength and destination. The last group contains standard containers, which
are divided into several weight classes. Firstly, containers with special requirements are
placed around the vessel following three heuristic rules. Next, containers from the 10th
group is placed into the remaining slots, while trying to maximize the intake of the ves-
sel. The third step includes two Integer Programming (IP) models that place the standard
containers into slots and optimize the stability. The fourth and ﬁnal step is using a local
search operator with a swap neighbourhood to optimize the trim and metacentric height
(GM) of the vessel. If the local search method was not able to ﬁnd any feasible solution,
the number of loaded containers is reduced with one, until all constraints were satisﬁed.
This could also inﬂuence to a larger computational time, since the local search proce-
dure needs to restart after each container removal. Furthermore, it is not beneﬁcial to
extend the transportation time for some containers by removing those from the load list.
The model does not directly handle overstowage, bending or lashing strength but only
considers constraints mentioned in step four. Since the model uses both Dynamic Pro-
gramming (DP) and IP models, it is expected to have long computational times.
In Aslidis (1984) the presented heuristic with local search minimizes the overstowage
and is limited to only consider one type of containers without any special requirements
or weight capacities. The ﬁrst stage of the heuristic assigns containers along the vessel
with respect to the trim using the ballast water. The use of ballast water will increase
the fuel consumption of the vessel and hereby the operational cost. In the second stage,
a two step local search with a swap neighbourhood is performed in order to solve the
GM constraints. First, by interchanging within a single stack, and if thus is not enough,
then by interchanging within the whole bay until locating a feasible solution. In the third
step, ﬁnal improvements are made to improve the GM without reducing the objective
value. It was possible to solve two instances of 1,540 TEU in a multi-port scenario within
a computational time of 30 seconds.
Liu et al. (2011) are some of the authors that have extended the block stowage heuris-
tic originally presented by Yoke et al. (2009), described in Subsection 4.1.1. The ﬁrst stage
obtains a large set of ”randomized” initial solutions, whereas the best from a multi objec-
tive perspective (overstowage, longest crane completion time and killed slots) are passed
on to the next stage. The second stage uses a Tabu Search (TS) with a swap neighbourhood
of containers with the same destination, which improves the stability of those solutions
from stage one that were not dominated. The use of TS makes it possible for the local
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 4
31
search method to escape the local minimum linked to each of the random initial solu-
tions. The use of TS can be rather time consuming. A real world case study of a 5,436
TEU vessel is solved within a computational time of 40 minutes providing a list of 10
non-dominant solutions from 500 generated ”random” initial solutions. It is then up to
the stowage planner to choose upon the non-dominant solution, depending on which of
the objectives that is most important. Low et al. (2011) did also improve the work of Yoke
et al. (2009) but with a focus point on weight limits, trim and heel, using a local search
with a swap operator.
Pacino and Jensen (2009) presented a three-phase placement heuristic with a local search
using a swap neighbourhood. Both reefer and non-reefer as well as both 20’ and 40’ con-
tainers are modelled but do not consider stability or GM in the formulation. The heuristic
takes four different objectives into account; overstowage, non-reefer containers in reefer
slots, the number of different discharge ports represented in a single stack and each used
stack. Each of those objectives are penalized by different weights. The ﬁrst phase is
a placement heuristic prioritizing reefers, then destination, and ﬁnally size and stowed
stack from the bottom choosing used stack before empty stacks. The second phase uses
a local search with a swap neighbourhood to obtain a feasible solution, while the third
phase improves the feasible solution also using a local search with a swap operator. 19
instances of sizes varying from 16 TEU up to 176 TEU have been solved all within a com-
putational time of 13.19 seconds, where 13 of those instances are solved to optimality.
The instances solved with the method are too small to be considered as real-life instance.
Thus, it is not clear how the performance of three-phase placement heuristic would be
for larger instances.
4.1.3
Approaches With Single Models
In this subsection, the literature of some of the other solution approaches to the CSP
would be reviewed. Lots of studies have been done with the focus of single models,
which handle the problem without decomposing it into smaller optimization problems.
4.1.3.1
Mathematical Programming
Avriel and Penn (1993) were the ﬁrst to use mathematical programming in order to solve
the CSP. In the article they present a 0-1 binary LP formulation. This formulation is able
to ﬁnd the optimal solution due to minimize the shifting (overstowage) for a single rect-
angular bay with a limited capacity, due to the high number of binary variables needed.
The formulation only considers the most general constraints, such as containers must be
loaded and supported from below. While constraints such as ship stability, hatch covers
or mixture of container sizes are not included in the formulation, but do though exist in
an extension of the model. Even though there are no computational results presented in
the article the authors claim to successfully have implemented the model in GAMS. Fur-
==PAGE:48==
32
LITERATURE REVIEW
thermore, the article presents two extensions of the mathematical model, which includes
different sizes of containers and hatch covers. These extensions are not implemented.
Since the model is only suitable to solve simple and small instances, the formulation is
combined with the WC-algorithm, which is also presented in the article and further de-
scribed in 4.1.1. Even though the WC-algorithm reduces the scale of the problem, the
formulation would only be able to solve the problem optimality for relatively small in-
stances despite the simplicity of the model.
Another mathematical programming approach to solve the CSP is presented in the ar-
ticle by Ambrosino et al. (2004), where a 0-1 LP model is presented with two types of
containers and three different weight classes. The model handles overstowage as a con-
straint and does not consider overstowage in the objective function, which instead aims
to minimize the loading time. The model handles the weight issue by forcing the heavier
containers to be loaded in the bottom of the stack, while the stability of the ship has to be
within certain limits between both the front-rear and left-right in terms of weight differ-
ence. In the study, 13 instances of a 198 TEU container ship are solved using the presented
model in maximum 34 minutes. Since the mathematical model considers a huge number
of the constraints associated with container arrangement (see Section 2.4 and Section 3.3),
the computational time is very high even for small instance as shown in the article.
Li et al. (2012) proposed a different 0-1 IP model for the CSP with both 20’ and 40’
standard containers and considering a multi-port scenario. The 0-1 IP model objective
function minimizes the overstowage. It does also consider the vertical, transverse and
longitudinal distribution of the ships weight, this is done by introducing the three factors
GM, heel and trim. In the model, the balance constraints are modelled in a similar way as
in the 0-1 LP model presented by Ambrosino et al. (2004), where the weight differences
of the ship has to be within a certain tolerance. Though the stability of the ship is mod-
elled as a constraint, the weight limitations are not considered. The 0-1 IP model has been
tested approximately 1,000 times with a randomly generated dataset of containers for a
ﬁctional ship with a capacity of 800 TEU visiting 10 ports. The computational results for
the IP model are not presented in the article, but the objective value, ship utilization and
weight balance tolerance is presented. Because no computational times are reported, it is
hard to evaluate the real performance of the IP model, compared to other IP models.
4.1.3.2
Bin Packing Heuristics
Both Sciomachen and Tanfani (2003) and Zhang et al. (2005) have related the Bin Packing
Problem (BPP) to the CSP. Sciomachen and Tanfani (2003) proposed a three dimensional
bin packing heuristic that exploits the potential of a 3D-BPP presented in Martello et al.
(2000). The proposed heuristic takes into account three standard size containers as well as
two types of high cube containers, where the overall objective is to minimizing the total
loading time of the vessel. According to the heuristic, the vessel is divided into sections
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 4
33
(bins), which are then ﬁlled with containers grouped by destination and weight classes.
Furthermore, each group of containers is sorted by increasing size and decreasing weight
in order to satisfy the weight and size constraint. The main sections are of a simple form,
which is easy to stow, while more oddly shaped sections are left out for later stowage.
Computational results are presented for both a 198 TEU vessel and a larger 1,800 TEU
ship. For the 198 TEU vessel 18 different instances are all solved within 1 second, while
two industrial instances with a 1,800 TEU vessel are solved in approximately 33 minutes.
The solutions are very depending on how the ship is divided into sections, why this is
a critical factor for the BBP to work properly. The method is swift compared to an exact
method and with an average gap of 10.75% for small instances. Further the method se-
cures feasibility, while lots of constraints are considered. The investigated instances, do
only consider a low number of ports.
Zhang et al. (2005) presented a bin packing model, which considers a multi-port scenario
for the stowage problem. The bin packing model minimizes the overall used number of
bays together with the number of shifts required, but the model does not consider nei-
ther stability constraints or special containers. The packing decisions are modelled as a
binary decision tree, traversed using the bin packing heuristic. One case study with an
895 TEU container vessel was reported, but the results were presented without any com-
putational times. Even though the instance is solved without any overstowage it is hard
to really evaluate the performance, since only one instance is solved and the time was not
reported.
4.1.3.3
Genetic Algorithms
Todd and Sen (1997) was the ﬁrst to implement a Genetic Algorithm (GA) for solving the
CSP. The idea is to represent the solution as a vector with different sector as a complete
encoding of the total solution. With an encoding like the one in Todd and Sen (1997) the
search space tends to be very large, which causes the algorithm to be very slow and inef-
ﬁcient. Furthermore, it is not possible to guarantee a feasible solution with the solutions
obtained by the crossover operator. As a result, it is necessary to use a repair method af-
terwards in order to obtain a feasible solution. This can be problematic, since this method
will destroy the information inheritance mechanism, which is the key for the GA.
In Dubrovsky et al. (2002) an improved GA to the one presented in Todd and Sen (1997)
with a compact encoding of the CSP was presented. It did succeed to signiﬁcantly reduce
the search space and storage resource consumption by only representing the changes of
the container arrangement between the ports instead of the total solution. Considering
containers of one size for a single bay without taking neither container weight or ves-
sel stability into account, the objective of the algorithm is to minimize the overstowage.
Two vessel sizes of 500 TEU and 1,000 TEU are considered visiting any number of ports
between 5 and 14 for 413 randomly generated instances. The computational time varies
==PAGE:50==
34
LITERATURE REVIEW
from a few seconds for some of the small instance up to approximate 1 hour for the larger
instances visiting many ports. The GA is compared to the SH-algorithm from Avriel et
al. (1998) and obtains results similar to heuristic approach.
Imai et al. (2006) proposed a GA that considers both container stowage and terminal
sequencing. The terminal sequencing is taken into account by modelling the transporta-
tion time for a container through the yard to the vessel. The storage of containers are
limited to only the hold of the vessel, however the authors do claim that the model can
be adapted to also include containers on deck. The model minimizes the total loading
time, while stability, trim, GM, heel angles and overstowage are modelled as constraints.
The model has very large computational times. The results and computational times of
solving 22 randomly generated instances with vessel capacity of 504 TEU and 2,016 TEU
are reported. It takes more than one hour to solve all of the instances. However, the au-
thors claim that the computational times can be reduced by as much as seven times by
removing the tournament of the GA and allowing worse results.
4.1.3.4
Constraint Programming
Ambrosino and Sciomachen (1998) was the ﬁrst to do an academic paper to solve the
Master Bay Planning Problem (MBPP) by using a constraint satisfaction approach. In the
implemented model, they did allow a relaxation of some of the constraints, which where
then handled by a heuristic approach. The model considers two container sizes, as well
as three different container types and different weights. It is claimed that the model pro-
vides good feasible solutions within 10 minutes of computational time for loading vessel
with a capacity of 350 TEU, but no results are reported.
Another Constraint Programming (CP) approach is proposed by Delgado et al. (2009),
where three different objectives are considered; minimize overstowage, keep stacks empty
if possible, only load non-reefers in reefer slots if absolute necessary. The model does con-
sider most of the stacking rules but does take neither International Maritime Organization
(IMO) restrictions nor stability into account. Additionally constraints such as channelling
constraints, symmetry breaking and partial evaluation are presented. In Delgado et al.
(2012b) this work has been improved, where most instances of 100 TEU vessels are solved
to optimality within 1 second. The model only considers the Slot Planning Phase (SPP)
and does not consider stability, since this is assumed to be handled in the Master Planning
Phase (MPP). The same approach is used in Pacino (2012) to evaluate Constraint Based
Local Search (CBLS) algorithm by generating optimal solutions.
4.1.3.5
Approaches Using Simulation
The ﬁrst use of simulation for solving the CSP was proposed by Shields (1984), where
Monte Carlo simulation was utilised for a Computer-Aided Planning System (CAPS). The
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 4
35
CAPS utilised the technique from Monte Carlo simulation to generate stowage plans as
well as handling the uncertainty of cargo load lists. The algorithm stows a group contain-
ers in a single bay by pruning slots in other bays and only leaving out the best alternative
bays. Given penalty functions, the best three solutions in each port are selected and then
used to obtain the overall best solution for all ports. There are not reported any perfor-
mance evaluation of the CAPS.
Later on Azevedo et al. (2012) presented another similar simulation approach to the
CSP as Shields (1984) but without consider stochasticity. Then, based on a set of loading-
and unloading rules, stowage plans are simulated from a solution search. In order to
reduce the search space, the authors use a beam search method. The model solves 15 ran-
domly generated instances varying from 10 to 30 ports for a 1,500 TEU vessel with com-
putational times between 400 and 18,300 seconds. All instances include only standard
containers of the same size and weight. Furthermore, the vessel stability is minimized
through the objective function as well as the number of container movements.
4.1.3.6
Alternative Approaches
It is not all approaches which is possible to ﬁt under one of the previously categories
in this subsection. Giemsch and Jellinghaus (2004) proposed one of these alternative
approaches, where a three-step heuristic is used. The ﬁrst step is inspired by the WC-
algorithm from Avriel et al (1993), where containers with the same destination are as-
signed to the same stack. The second step tries to place the remaining containers without
creating overstowage. The third step is using a heuristic branch and bound search to
minimize the number of necessary shifts. There are not presented any results, but the
authors do claim to improve 1038 out of 1050 instances compared to the SH-algorithm
from Avriel et al. (1998). However the model does not consider container weights, vessel
stability or different container sizes or types. The authors do suggest that it is possible to
make an adjustment for including the stability constraint.
Nugroho et al. (2004) presented another alternative approach with a historical cased
based solution method. This method used data from previously generated stowage plans
to generate new stowage plans. Hereby, the main idea is that the method would improve
over time, since the database would grow and a learning ability was implied into the
method. Since every single planning problem is unique, the solution approach in Nu-
groho et al. (2004) can be adapted for all problems and is not based on either mathemati-
cal model or ”if-then” rules. It is though hard to evaluate to approach, since there are not
reported any results for the method.
==PAGE:52==
36
LITERATURE REVIEW
4.1.4
Decomposition Models
Instead of using a single model approach to the CSP some authors have decomposed the
problem. Botter and Brinati (1992) presented a decomposition of a detailed mathematical
model into one sub-problem for assigning containers to slots that minimize the over-
stowage and another sub-problem for the loading sequence of those containers. Since
the solution for the assignment problem is found ﬁrst, it can be used to highly reduce the
number of variables for the sequencing problem. Additionally, type, origin, destination
and weight class group containers in order to reduce the number of variables. The de-
composition is claimed to solve small instances but without reporting any results. Since
the decomposition had high computational times, a ”branch and bound” search was in-
troduced. It is claimed that the branch and bound search had reasonable computational
times and good solution quality, yet again without presenting any results.
Wilson and Roach (1999) were the ﬁrst to propose a decomposition model based on
blocks, which are strategically located around the vessel. These blocks follow the struc-
tural design of the vessel and are either locate below- or above deck in order to follow the
pattern of the hatch-covers. The decomposition ﬁrst deal with the assignment of contain-
ers into these block, so the containers are grouped by classes. The ﬁrst sub-problem of the
decomposition then minimizes the hatch overstowage, maximizes crane utilization and
grouping as many containers with the same destination in the same block. In the second
sub-problem, a Tabu Search assigns containers to speciﬁc slots within each of the blocks,
by only considering the assignment within a single block the search space is reduced for
the algorithm. This assignment minimizes the overstowage and place as many containers
with the same discharge port in the same stack as possible, while considering weights.
The authors claim to solve an instance of a 688 TEU vessel with a computational time of
90 minutes of the block assignment and solutions for each block was conducted in under
an hour. However, there are not presented any results. The authors did then present the
same approach again in both Wilson and Roach (2000) and Wilson and Roach (2001).
Kang and Kim (2002) did also present a decomposition solution approach to the CSP
inspired by the one introduced in Wilson and Roach (1999). In Kang and Kim (2002),
containers are ﬁrst grouped and then being assigned to different blocks in the ﬁrst part
decomposition problem. For this part the objective is to minimize overstowage and sta-
bility violations, while solving one port at the time. This solution is then past on to the
second part decomposition problem. The second part then solves the slot assignment
within each of the blocks using a tree search enumeration for each of the blocks. The
solution for the slot assignment problem is then passed back to the ﬁrst sub-problem in
order to ﬁnd any improvements. Computational results for 180 random instances con-
sisting of either four, six or eight ports, a vessel capacity varying between 2,500 TEU,
3,000 TEU and 4,000 TEU, while considering two or three different weight classes of only
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 4
37
one size of containers are solved using at most 640 seconds.
Ambrosino et al. (2006) proposed another decomposition approach, where a three-phase
decomposition is used. The overall objective of the decomposition is to minimize the to-
tal loading time while overstowage is modelled as a constraint. In the ﬁrst decomposition
phase containers are grouped by their discharge port and loaded onto a single bay. In the
second phase an IP model is used to solved the slot assignment within each single bay
considering two types of containers and both the stability- and weight constraints. The
last and third phase is using a local search heuristic with the neighbourhood of a swap
move to improve the stability for the vessel. The decomposition approach was used to
solve the same instances as in Ambrosino et al. (2004), see subsubsection 4.1.3.1, where
it was possible to ﬁnd solutions within 1.05% limit of the previous results in 4 minutes,
which is an improvement of 60% in terms of computational time.
The same authors proposed some modiﬁcations to the decomposition approach in Am-
brosino et al. (2009). The main idea of both the ﬁrst- and second phase of the decompo-
sition remain unchanged, though a new IP model was used to solve the slot assignment.
In the third phase a Tabu Search replaced the local search method. These modiﬁcations
did make it possible to solve instances of 2,124 TEU vessel with a computational time in
74.4 seconds.
Ambrosino et al. (2010) presented another modiﬁcation to the decomposition approach,
where the assignment of container groups to different bays remains unchanged. Then
a construction heuristic using an Ant Colony Optimization (ACO) is implemented, where
containers are ﬁrst assigned to a stack and then to a speciﬁc slot within each bay. Af-
terwards a local search method is used to improve the solution. Finally the ant sys-
tem (pheromone trail) is updated and the whole process is repeated until the ACO has
reached convergence. The model does neither take overstowage or multi-port solutions
into account. It was possible to solve instances of a 5,632 TEU vessel with a computa-
tional time of 139.4 seconds.
Gumus et al. (2008) presented another different decomposition approach in four stages.
In the ﬁrst stage a part of a single bay is assigned to a group of containers with the same
origin and destination. The second makes an allocation decision for tiers, which is ex-
plicit done with the focus of handling hatch-covers. The third stage of the decomposition
uses the output from stage two to assign containers to slots. In the fourth stage containers
are assigned to slots with respect to the hydro-static constraint. The authors claim that
the approach is scalable for almost any sizes, and had achieved good results for instances
with industrial data. However, there are not reported any computational results.
==PAGE:54==
38
LITERATURE REVIEW
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 5
Solution Method
In this chapter the solution methods for the Container Stowage Planning (CSP) used in
this thesis will be explained. The chapter will go through how the Main algorithm devel-
oped by Ding and Chou (2015) can be used as a solution approach to the CSP. The Main
algorithm is furthermore extended to contain a local search (LS) algorithm, which aims to
decrease the number of shifts needed. Finally the chapter presents an extension to the
Main algorithm that implements a weight constraint for each stack in the stowage plan
considering a 3-step heuristic algorithm.
5.1
Solution Approach
Solution z is formulated as a dynamic list of stowage plans αp ∈{1, ...N −1}, one for
each port N along the route of a container vessel except the last one. There is not gen-
erated any stowage plan for the last port N, since it is assumed that all containers are
unloaded in port N. A stowage plan for port p is given as αp and consists of a single
rectangular bay. Given the set of stacks s ∈S and the set of tiers per stack t ∈T, the
capacity of the rectangular bay and hereby the number of slots in the stowage plan are
given as P = S · T.
In the representation of a stowage plan, the set of tiers in each stack is given as a dynamic
list of containers, t ∈Ts, where Ts is the number of containers loaded into stack s. xst
deﬁnes the container, ciju loaded onto stack s in tier t. xst can be chosen from the set all
containers c ∈C, where C is all containers either already loaded onto the vessel or from
the load list lp for port p. Each container ciju is origin in port i, destined to port j and has
u = 1 if it is loaded in port port p, where i ≤p < j and u = 0 otherwise. Later on a
container is said to be tagged if u = 1 and untagged if u = 0.
The objective value π of the total solution z is given as the total number of shifts needed
along the route of a container vessel. πNS
p
is the number of necessary shifts (NS) in stowage
plan αp and πVS
p
is the number of voluntary shifts (VS) in αp. The objective value for αp is
a sum of all shifts needed and is given as; πp = πNS
p
+ πVS
p . Finally the objective value
for solution z is given as a sum of all needed shifts for all generated stowage plans along
39
==PAGE:56==
40
SOLUTION METHOD
the route of a container vessel; π =
N−1
∑
p=1
πp.
Each of the implemented algorithms dynamically generate N −1 stowage plans starting
with the one corresponding to the ﬁrst port in the route and then sequentially generating
rest of the stowage plans. Once a stowage plan is generated with one of the algorithms,
it is added to the existing solution z.
5.2
The Ding And Chou (2015) Algorithm
Ding and Chou (2015) did present an extended algorithm of the Suspensory Heuristic Al-
gorithm (SH). This algorithm solves the simplest version of the CSP without considering
any other constraints other than the capacity constraints. It does hereby not satisfy any
of the safety rules associated to container stowage or stacking rules, which are described
in Chapter 2 and Section 3.3. The objective of the algorithm is to minimize the number of
shifts for each stowage plan for a container vessel visiting N ports.
The capacity constraints for each of the stacks are the only constraints considered when
placing containers. The Main algorithm is obviously not applicable for real life scenarios
because this would create serious safety problems for both the vessel and the cargo. The
algorithm is implemented in Java with a CPLEX 12.1 solver in order to evaluate the per-
formance as well as the possibility of expansion options for the Main algorithm in Ding
and Chou (2015).
Algorithm 1 : Placement Heuristic from Ding and Chou (2015)
1: Initialize an empty vessel
2: for all ports p ∈{1, ..., N −1} do
3:
Unload p-containers and those blocking them and update lp
4:
Sort lp in a descending order of container destination
5:
for all containers ciju ∈lp do
6:
Load container ciju based on 10 placement rules from the bottom and up on the
vessel
7:
end for
8: end for
Line 1 does refer to the initialization of an empty bay on the vessel in port 1. Line 2 of
the algorithm states that the vessel visits all ports along the route in an ascending order.
Next in Line 3 all containers in the stowage plan destined for the current visited port, p as
well as blocking containers are unloaded . All unloaded containers not destined to port
p are then added to the trying set or load list, lp. lp is a set of all containers that need to
be loaded onto the vessel but have not yet been assigned to a speciﬁc slot at the vessel.
Thereby does the size of lp decrease by one if a container is loaded and likewise increased
by one if a container is shifted. In Line 4 lp is sorted in a descending order based on the
sequence number of discharge port in the route. Line 5 goes through all containers in lp
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 5
41
one by one. Finally in Line 6 the next container from the load list is assigned to a slot
in the vessel, loading from the bottom and up. This assignment of containers is based
on 10 placement rules, which are based on the current state of the stowage plan and the
containers in lp.
Placement Rules
Rule 1 update the state of which container destination, j, to be loaded next in a descend-
ing order based on the status of the load list, lp. Rule 2 and Rule 3 are designed to handle
a stowage plan, where only a single stack has not reached the capacity limit. Rule 4
assigns N-containers to either empty- or N-top stacks. Rule 5 places j-containers into
in-order stacks with a j-container on top. Rule 6 places j-containers into empty stacks.
In Rule 7 containers are placed into in-order l-top (l > j) stacks without creating further
overstowage. Rule 8 deals with a stowage plan, where a l-min stack (l ≥j) is out-of-
order but the j-container can be placed on top without creating further overstowage. In
Rule 9 containers are placed in free space without considering the state of the stack. It
is considered whether or not it is possible to perform a VS (further described in the fol-
lowing paragraph) to avoid costlier shift at future ports. Rule 10 is the last and ﬁnal
rule, securing that all containers are loaded in a feasible way with no consideration of the
objective function.
Myopic Voluntary Shifting Determination (MVSD)
One of the key factors in Ding and Chou (2015) is the Myopic Voluntary Shifting Deter-
mination (MVSD), which determines the need of VS to prevent costlier shifts in future
ports. The MVSD consider the best reordering level m for a stack given the current state
of both the load list and the examined stack, where m ∈{k −1, ..., u}, k is the nearest des-
tined port and u is furthest destined port for a container in the load list, lpi. A reordered
stack up to m exists if there is no blocked v-container with v ≤m in it. A stack is said
to be in order up to m, if all v-containers with v > m are stowed under those containers
with v ≤m.
B (m) is deﬁned as the number of overstowage containers after reordering up to m, where
B (0) is the state before any movements. Furthermore G (m) is the necessary number of
unloading movements needed in order to reorder up to m and H (m) is the number of
containers loaded from the load list at a reordering level m.
The reordering level for a stack is chosen with this procedure: Choose the largest H (m),
then the smallest G (m) + B (m) upon the largest H (m) followed by the smallest B (m) of
the remaining options and ﬁnally the smallest m is chosen.
5.2.1
Evaluation Of The Ding And Chou (2015) Algorithm
The algorithm proposed in Ding and Chou (2015) is to the author’s knowledge, the best-
proposed simple placement heuristic. Based on the presented results obtained with the
==PAGE:58==
42
SOLUTION METHOD
Main algorithm in the study, the authors claim that it is better than the SH-algorithm from
Avriel et al. (1998). The results show that the Main algorithm is both faster in computa-
tional time and obtains better solution in terms of minimization of the number of shifts
needed. In average an instance with an authentic transport matrix, a vessel capacity
of 10,000 and a route of 20 ports can be solved within 10 seconds with the Main algo-
rithm. It is in other word possible to ﬁnd feasible solutions within a reasonable amount
of computational time for all instances with a feasible transport matrix. Additionally the
Main algorithm does not only obtain feasible solutions but also solutions of good quality.
For almost every single set-up it obtains an average shifting ratio below 1% and in most
cases far lower. This is mainly due to the introduction of the MVSD (see Section 5.2),
which makes VS possible and thereby signiﬁcantly lower the number of shifts needed.
There are some drawback of the algorithm in Ding and Chou (2015). The algorithm does
only consider the capacity constraints and no other constraints are taken into account,
which is a huge simpliﬁcation. The fact that no stacking rules, stability or stress values
are taken into account through the developed set of placement rules or either as second
step of the algorithm limits the possible use of the algorithm. As the problem is signif-
icantly simpliﬁed, it is also expected that solutions obtained with the Main algorithm
needs to be of better quality than if several other constraints were considered.
5.3
Genereal Local Search
For optimization problem a local search method is used to iteratively go through a number
of candidate solutions with the purpose of improving the objective value. A local search is
often used to solve hard optimization problems, where it is not easy to ﬁnd a good initial
solution.
In Figure 5.1 a ﬂowchart is presented for a general local search procedure. A local search
procedure is also known as an improvement heuristic, which improves the existing solution
z until the algorithm is terminated. Termination criteria can be a local optimum, a ﬁxed
number of iterations or a time limit. The algorithm iteratively ﬁnds a new solution z′
within the neighbourhood N (z) of the so far best solution. The neighbourhood is a smaller
search space of the of the total solution space containing a ﬁnite set of solution, N (z).
N (z) to solution z is all solutions that can be reached by performing a change/ a move
on the existing solution to obtain a new solution, z′. If the algorithm iteratively chooses
the best solution z′ within N (z) and not just any improving z′ in N (z), then the method
is known as a hill climber. The local search algorithm is repeated until no better solutions
exists within N (z) and the returned solution is a local optimum. The number of solu-
tions in N (z) depend on the move applied on the solution z. Different moves will give
different neighbourhoods and neighbourhood sizes, which in the end often give differ-
ent solutions. The bigger the neighbourhood is the higher is the change that the optimal
solution is within the neighbourhood. A bigger neighbourhood will require a higher
computational time. It is hereby crucial to deﬁne the right size of the neighbourhood,
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 5
43
Initial solution z
Find a solution
z′ ∈N (z),
is π′ better than π?
Return z
Local optimum
z ←z′
No
Yes
Figure 5.1: Local search ﬂowchart
where a trade-off between objective value and computational time is considered.
5.3.1
Implemented Local Search For The Ding And Chou (2015) Algorithm
One of the drawbacks to the algorithm by Ding and Chou (2015) is that it is a pure place-
ment heuristic, where one bad placement of a container can cause extra overstowage and
inﬂuence the total result.
Due to this the Main algorithm is extended with a local search (LS) heuristic algorithm in
order to catch eventually bad placed containers. The LS algorithm is therefore done for
each stowage plan along the route of the vessel after the Main algorithm has generated
a initial stowage plan. Some instances contain a very high number of containers as de-
scribed in Section 5.5, in order to keep the required number of operations at a minimum,
the LS algorithm is limited to consider tagged containers. Therefore when a vessel berths
a new port, all containers already on the vessel is set to be untagged. If a container from
the load list is loaded onto the vessel, this container is set as a tagged container. Further
if one of the containers already on the vessel is temporarily removed from the vessel and
then loaded again, this container would also be set as a tagged container.
The LS algorithm is performed after all containers in the load list lp have been assigned
to a slot on the vessel, where only containers set to be tagged are considered by the algo-
rithm. The implemented LS algorithm uses a swap move of two containers as the neigh-
==PAGE:60==
44
SOLUTION METHOD
bourhood. The swap move is carried out regardless of the vessel utilization but only
when there exists at least one overstowage container in the stowage plan.
The LS algorithm starts with the initial stowage plan αp for port p and is presented in
Algorithm 2.
Line 1 initializes the set O ⊆S of all stacks out of order. Next Line 2 determines the
termination criteria for the LS algorithm. It is either terminated, when all stacks are in
order (O = Ø), when the computational time limit of 10 minutes has been reached or
when all possible swaps has been checked. Line 3 goes through all possible slots for the
ﬁrst container swap option, the ﬁrst swap option must be a container in a stack s ∈O,
while Line 4 selects the container if it is tagged. Line 5 goes through all possible slots for
the second swap option, the second swap option can be a container from all stacks s ∈S
and Line 6 selects the container if it is tagged. Line 7 to 11 performs a delta evaluation
of the two chosen swap options. The two containers are swapped, if the swap improves
the objective value πp. Finally Line 12 restarts the algorithm if an improving swap has
been found. After the delta evaluation has been executed, it is checked whether or not to
terminate. If the LS algorithm is terminated because of all stacks are in order the stowage
plan is optimal. If it is terminated, since all possible swap options have been checked or
the time limit has been reached, the stowage plan is a local minimum.

2: while O ̸= Ø ∧time < timelimit ∧allSwapsChecked = f alse do
3:
for all (s, t) ∈{(s, t)|s ∈O, t ∈Ts}
4:
if u = 1 then select ciju ∈xst
5:
for all (s′, t′) ∈{(s′, t′)|s′ ∈S, t′ ∈Ts′}

5.4
Algorithm With Weight Constraint
In order to see, whether the algorithm presented in Ding and Chou (2015) is applicable of
handling more constraints than the capacity constraints. The stack weight constraints has
been added to the original mathematical formulation proposed in Avriel et al. (1998). A
3-step heuristic algorithm has been implemented with the purpose of obtaining feasibility
by satisfying Constraint 5.1 as well as the other constraints from Section 3.4.
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 5
45
Ts
∑
t=1
W(xst) ≤ωs∀s ∈S
(5.1)
After the mathematical model is updated to include the stack weight limit for each stack
ωs, the Main algorithm from Section 5.2 is not able of obtaining feasible solutions, since
it does not consider the container weights at all. Therefore it is tested whether or not, it
is possible to use the 3-step algorithm to generate feasible stowage plans of good quality.
As the Main algorithm is able of generating stowage plans with a reasonable number of
shifts, it is chosen to keep it as a construction heuristic. The initial stowage plan is then
modiﬁed by the 3-step algorithm in order to obtain feasibility. Hereby does the 3-step
algorithm modiﬁes the stowage plans after containers have been assigned to slots with
Algorithm 1 in each port, p.
Algorithm 3 is the ﬁrst algorithm of the 3-step, which is a local search with a swap neigh-
bourhood. It tries to swap tagged containers with the same discharge port in order to
minimize the number of heavy stacks without creating any new overstowage containers.
The local search is only executed if there exists any stacks with a total weight greater than
the allowed limit for each stack, ωs. First in Line 1 the set H ⊆S is initialized containing
all stacks with a weight exceeding ωs. Next Line 3 states that the algorithm is iterated
as long as there exists heavy stacks, the time limit has not been reached or all swaps are
not checked. The time limit for the local search at each port is divided equally between
all ports on the route. The time limit is furthermore divided by 2, since two local search
methods should be executed within the time limit. If all of the total time limit is not used
for port p, there will be extra time for the local search in the subsequent port p + 1. It is
assumed that the running time of both the placement heuristic in Algorithm 1 and the re-
location heuristic for tagged containers Algorithm 4 is close to 0. If one of the mentioned
conditions reach the termination criteria, the algorithm is terminated.
Line 5 and 6 locate the ﬁrst swap option, which has to be a tagged container from the set
h ∈H of heavy stacks. Line 7 and 8 locate and select a tagged container as the second
swap option, which must be chosen from a not heavy stack s ∈S \ H. Additionally Line
8 secures that the second swap option is also a tagged container with the same destina-
tion, as the ﬁrst swap option j = j′. Furthermore does Line 8 ensures that the second
swap option is lighter than the ﬁrst swap option, in order to lower the total weight of the
heavy stack, h. Finally Line 9 evaluates whether the new total weight of the not heavy
stack s exceeds the weight limit ωs. If the stack weight limit of s is not violated then Line
10 executes the swap of the ﬁrst- and second swap option, xht and xst′.

swap(xht, xst′)
The second phase of the 3-step algorithm is a local search with a relocation neighbour-
hood presented in Algorithm 4. The aim of this local search is to relocate containers onto
other stacks, where neither the weight nor capacity has reached the predeﬁned limits.
The algorithm start by initializes the set H ⊆S as all stacks s ∈S that exceeds ωs in
Line 1. In order to be able to relocate a container, it is necessary that all slots are not
occupied with containers, therefore the conducted stowage plan must have either empty
stacks E0 ⊆S or eligible stacks E ⊆S. Therefore are these sets initialized in Line 2 and 3
before the algorithm starts. Line 5 starts the algorithm if there exist heavy stacks and free
spaces in the stowage plan, while Line 6 ﬁnds the container to relocate onto another stack
among all containers in all heavy stacks. Next Line 7 initializes the set EN ⊆S containing
all eligible N-top stacks, the set is updated to set E′
N in Line 8 to contain all those stacks
from EN, which has weight capacity for the container chosen as the relocation option.
A number of prioritised rules determines, which stack the chosen relocation option must
be relocated onto. The chosen stack depends on the discharge port j of the relocation
option and the state of the stowage plan, αp. The ﬁrst two rules are stated in Line 10 and
Line 13, if the discharge port of the relocation option is equal to N. It can therefore either
be relocated to a N-top or a random empty stack if such exists. If possible the relocate
option is relocated to the top of the largest N-top stack in the set s ∈EN and otherwise
to a random empty stack s ∈E0. Line 16 chooses a stack from the set of eligible stack
E ⊆S, while the destination of the relocation option is j < N. The set of eligible stacks is
updated in Line 17 to only contain stacks with weight capacity for the chosen relocation
option. Line 18 goes through all possible slots (s, t′) with tagged containers in the update
set of eligible stacks E′. In Line 20 to 22 the chosen relocation option is relocated into
stack s ∈E′ on top of the relocation option xst′ if j = j′. If no container with destination
j = j′ exists in E′, then Line 24 checks whether the relocation option can be relocation
in between two containers. Where the container below is destined to a later port a > j
and the container above is destined for an earlier port j > b. If such stack exists, then the
relocation option is relocate to the slot above container xst′ in Line 25 to 27. Finally if the
relocation option is the container with the latest destined port in the heavy stack h and

The two ﬁrst parts of the 3 algorithm do take account for the objective value. As Algo-
rithm 3 and 4 do only consider the possibilities, where both containers are tagged and
are not actually loaded. Therefore changes considering only tagged containers do not
inﬂuence the number of shifts made. For lots of instance, this is not enough to obtain a
feasible stowage plan. The ﬁnal part of the 3-step algorithm uses a local search with a
swap neighbourhood presented in Algorithm 5.
As in Algorithm 3 the algorithm is only executed if there exists stacks, which exceeds
ωs. The set h ∈H of all heavy stacks initializes in Line 1. Line 3 deﬁnes the stopping
criteria for the 3-step algorithm similar to the one in Algorithm 3. The time limit is only

The ﬁrst swap option is chosen from one of the stacks in set h ∈H in Line 4 but the ﬁrst
swap option xht is now chosen freely within stack h, starting with the top container. In
Line 5 and 6 the second swap option is selected from the set of not heavy stacks, s ∈S \ H
and with a weight lower than the ﬁrst swap option. Finally the same criterion as in Al-
gorithm 3 evaluates and executes the swap of the two swap options in Line 7, where the
stack weight of stack s may not exceed ωs after the swap. The swap heuristic for all con-
tainers might bring some stacks out of order, since overstowage is not considered at all,
when swaps are evaluated. Therefore the set of stacks out of order, θ ⊆S, initializes in
Line 9. Afterwards, the 3-step algorithm executes the MVSD procedure in Line 12 for all
stacks s′ ∈θ.
5.5
Transportation Matrices
The instances used in this thesis are constructed from the data generation method pre-
sented in Ding and Chou (2015). The dataset is randomly generated, so a wide spread
of different transportation matrices are represented. The transportation matrices are di-
vided into three different types of matrices; long distance matrices, mix matrices and short
distance matrices. These types of transportation matrices were ﬁrst considered in Avriel et
al. (1998) but are also used in Ding and Chou (2015). None of these types of matrices are
fully loaded at any time. A transportation matrix τ = [Tij] is a (N −1) · (N −1) matrix
containing information of containers with origin in port i and destination in port j, it is
assumed that the transportation matrix is known in advance. Tij is an upper triangular
matrix, where Tij = 0 for all i ≥j. For a single rectangular bay, S is the total number of
stacks and T is the total number of tiers and P = S · T is the total capacity of the bay.
==PAGE:65==
CHAPTER 5
49
In long distance matrices most containers within the transportation matrix have a des-
tination far from the origin port. Equation 5.2 ensures that there are fewer containers
with a shorter transportation distance, than if the distance between origin and destina-
tion is longer.
The second type of transportation matrices are mix transportation matrices that contain
an even distribution of containers with destination shortly after the origin port and con-
tainers with a long distance between the origin- and destination port. In order to secure a
fairly even distribution of the distances between the origin and destination, mix matrices
must satisﬁed Equation 5.3.
The ﬁnal type of transportation matrices is short distance matrices, where this type of
transportation matrices contain a larger number of containers with a short distance be-
tween the loading port and unloading port. Assigning numbers in the transportation
matrices in lexicographic order generates these matrices.

5.5.1
Data Description
For this thesis a dataset of 360 different instances/ transportation matrices have been auto
generated with an even distribution of long, mix and short transportation matrices. It is
not only the type of matrices which vary, but also the number of stacks and tiers at the
vessel as well as the number of ports visited along the route. The capacity of the vessel is
generated, so the number of stacks and tiers depend on each other. Both the stacks and
tiers takes ﬁve different values among the different instances. Combining these different
values for the stacks and tiers, there exist eight different vessel capacities among the 360
instances. For each of the different vessel capacities, 45 different transportation matrices
are generated, among these do 15 instances consist of a route of respectively 5, 8 and 10
ports, N. These 15 instances are evenly distributed with ﬁve of each of the three different
transportation matrix types. Besides the constraints associated with the different types of
transportation matrices, they must all fulﬁl Equation 5.4, ensuring that the transportation
matrix is feasible.
Validation Of Transport Matrices
In Table 5.1 it can be observed that the average transportation distance for each container,
measured as the difference between the destination and origin port of each container.
Each port distance in Table 5.1 refers to the average of 40 different instance all with the
same matrix type and N.

As one could expect the average port distance for each container increases with the num-

Table 5.1, where the average port distance for each container is longest for long type ma-
trices, then mix type matrices and ﬁnally shortest for short type matrices.
The transportation matrix generator and the dataset has been implemented by Pacino.

• Capacity of 500 with 10 tiers and 50 stacks

evenly for all three kinds of transport matrices and the three different numbers of route
lengths.
Furthermore it is worth to mention that the container vessel Tripel E from
Maersk is limited to carrying 19 containers in the height, 10 below deck and nine above
deck. Therefore is the use of stacks with 40 tiers not realistic.

one port to another.

Chapter 6
Performance Analysis
This chapter presents an overview of the computational results obtained by the different
solution approaches presented in Chapter 5. Results from the implemented mathemat-
ical model in Section 3.4 will be presented in this chapter, followed by a presentation
of results from the placement heuristic proposed by Ding and Chou (2015) described in
Section 5.2 as the Main algorithm. Additionally results related to the with a local search
method from Subsection 5.3.1 known as the LS algorithm and results from the extended 3-
step algorithm with a stack weight constraint from Section 5.4 are presented in this chapter.
For all tables in this chapter the column Type groups all instances with the same trans-
portation matrix and the column P groups by vessel capacity, Shifts, π, are the average
objective and thereby the sum of necessary shifts (NS) and voluntary shifts (VS) for all feasi-
ble solutions. Feasible gives the percentage of instances within the considered group with
a feasible solution. Time refers to the average computational time either in (s.) seconds
or (ms.) milliseconds. Gap is the average solution quality compared to a lower bound
L obtained with Optimization Programming Language (OPL) using IBM OLOG CPLEX
Optimization Studio. Shifting Ratio (SR) is the relative performance of the stowage plan
SR = π/2K, where K is the number of containers in the transportation matrix for the
given instance.
All solution approaches solves the same dataset of 360 instances further described in
Section 5.5 with a maximum computational time of 10 minutes. All experiments is run
on an Intel (R) Xeon (R) with a 2.30 Ghz processor and 16 GB RAM.
6.1
Performanca Of Mathematical Model From Avriel Et. Al (1998)
The mathematical model presented in Section 3.4 is written in OPL and solved with a
time limit of 10 minutes. The software is not applicable of solving all of the instances to
optimality within the time limit. In Table 6.1 to Table 6.6 the results from the mathemati-
cal model is presented.

with two feasible solution for P = 12, 000. The overall average number of shifts are rather
low, since the maximum average objective value is 1.1 when P = 3, 000. When the math-
ematical model does provide feasible solution it is in most cases also an optimal solution.

mathematical model. A higher N lower the solution quality and the number of feasible
solutions, while the computational time in average is increased with N. Finally there are
a slightly tendency that instances with a long transportation matrix are more likely to be
solved by the mathematical model than the others.
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 6
57
6.2
Performance Of Algorithm From Ding And Chou (2015)
The Main algorithm from Ding and Chou (2015) presented in Section 5.2 has been imple-
mented in Java with a CPLEX 12.1 solver. It solves the same dataset and has the same time
limit as with the mathematical model. The Main algorithm has further been extended to
the LS algorithm, which includes a local search with a swap neighbourhood, see Subsec-
tion 5.3.1 for further description. The results for both the Main-, the LS algorithm and
a summary of the results from the Math model in Section 6.1 is presented in Table 6.8 to
Table 6.13. In the tables (∗) refers to the fact that the mathematical model has not found
a feasible solution for all instances, while (◦) refers to not all feasible solutions are solved
to optimality. The computational times for both the Main- and LS algorithm are pre-
sented separately in Table 6.7. Finally those instances improved by the LS algorithm will
be presented with additional details in Table 6.14. Tables in this section presents either
average shifting values and worst case shifting in parentheses or the average shifting ra-
tio. The tables presents results for one type of transportation matrix, where these results
are grouped by both N and P.
Computational Times
Table 6.7 presents the computational times for both the Main- and LS algorithm. The ﬁrst
to notice is that the average computational times for both algorithms are presented in
milliseconds and not seconds as in Section 6.1. Both algorithms are as one could expect
way faster, than the proposed mathematical model since none of the computational times
exceeds 1 second. This is way lower than the computational times for the mathematical
model, which in many cases uses the total time limit. In Table 6.7 it can be observed
that the average computational times increases with the vessel capacity and thereby the
number of containers needed to be loaded. N do on the other hand not have a clear effect
on the running time for the Main algorithm. While the computational times increases
with N for the LS algorithm when P is large. As the average computational time is very
small, there are some uncertainty for the presented times in Table 6.7.

Long Transportation Matrices
Table 6.8 presents the results for all long transportation matrices. As it can be seen both
the Main- and LS algorithm are applicable of obtaining feasible solutions for all 360 in-
==PAGE:74==
58
PERFORMANCE ANALYSIS
stances in the dataset, while the Math model lacks to generate feasible solutions for lots
of instances. Table 6.8 shows that both the Main- and LS algorithm solves all instances
with N = 5 to optimality with one single exception, which has an objective value of 10
for both algorithms. The two implemented algorithms do hereby obtain as good results
as the Math model when N = 5, further does both algorithms provide optimal solutions
for large vessel capacities, which is not solvable for the Math model within the time limit.
For instances with N = 8 the average objective value for both the Main- and LS algo-
rithm are fairly higher than those obtained with the Math model. It should however be
noticed that the Math model is not applicable of obtaining a feasible solutions for lots of
instances. The average number of shifts needed with both the Main- and LS algorithm
are fairly the same for all capacity groups when N = 8. The worst case shifting value
does however increases signiﬁcantly with the capacity, and hereby is the spread higher
of the objective values for larger capacities. Further it can be observed that the average
shifting value is improved for instances with P = 500 and P = 3, 000 with respectively
0.2 and 0.8 in average shifting value. It is the worst case instance, which has decreased
the number of shifts for both improvements.
For instances with N = 10, the Math model is not able of generating any feasible solu-
tions for large or mid-size vessel capacities. Neither does it ﬁnds optimal solutions for
those solved. When examine the obtained objective values for both the Main- and LS al-
gorithm, the obtained objective values are signiﬁcantly higher than instances with N = 5
and N = 8, where most objective values are more than doubled. Finally one can observe
that the LS algorithm manages to decrease the objective value for six out of eight capac-
ity groups with an average decreasing from 0.2 to 3.2 as the biggest improvement in the
objective value.

Mix Transportation Matrices
Table 6.10 presents the results for all instances with mix transport matrices. As in Ta-
ble 6.8 it can be observed that both the Main- and LS algorithm obtains feasible solutions
for all instances, while the Math model only obtains a limited number of feasible solu-
tions. First it can be noticed that all instances with N = 5 are solved to optimality with
both the Main- and LS algorithm, while the Math model only solves those with P ≤1, 500
to optimality. As the Main algorithm manages to solve all instances with N = 5 to opti-
mality, there is logically no improvements to be found for the LS algorithm.
The average number of shifts for N = 8 are in most cases lower than those from Table 6.8.
In fact all instance with P = 4, 000 and P = 8, 000 are solved to optimality with the Main-
and LS algorithm opposite the Math model, which does not manages to ﬁnd feasible so-
lutions for any of those instances. Unlike the results from Table 6.8 both the average and
the worst case shifting value increases with the vessel capacity. The LS algorithm does
only improve one average shifting value for instances with N = 8 and P = 1, 500 with a
decreased of 1.2 to an average of 3.2.
Average shifting values for instances with N = 10 are low compared to the presented
ones for long transport matrices. The average shifting value is only above 5 shifts for
three out of the eight different capacities, where the results for these three capacities are
heavily inﬂuenced by single instances with a very high objective value. The LS algorithm
does improve the average shifting for four different capacities. These improvements are
however quite small and only range from an average improvement of 0.2 up to 1.2 as
the biggest improvement. As in Table 6.8 it is mainly the worst case instances, which is
improved by the LS algorithm.

Short Transportation Matrices
As for the two other types of transportation matrices, both algorithms manage to ﬁnd
feasible solutions for all instances with a short transportation matrix shown in Table 6.12.
For N = 5 the Math model solves all the smaller instances to optimality, but for P = 8, 000
one instance is not solved to optimality. On the other hand do both algorithms manage
to ﬁnd optimal solutions for all those instances with N = 5.
The Math model obtains even more limited results for N = 8, where only instance of
P = 500 and P = 1, 500 are solved to optimality, while only few feasible and not optimal
solutions exists for instances of P = 3, 000. The two algorithms do obtain almost optimal
solutions for all instances with N = 8. There is only one instance, which is not solved to
==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 6
61
optimality but with an objective value of 2. The LS algorithm has further not been able
to improve this instance even though it is not optimal.
For instances with N = 10, the Math model solves all instances with P = 500 and P =
1, 500, where only one is not solved to optimality. Additionally does the Math model
manages to ﬁnd a feasible but not optimal solution for one more instance, when P =
3, 000. The Main- and LS algorithm do solve almost every single instance to optimality
leaving only one instance not optimal with 2 shifts required when N = 10.

6.2.1
Improved Instances
From the results presented in Table 6.8, Table 6.10 and Table 6.12 it is clear that the effect
of the LS algorithm is limited. The objective value is only improved for 14 different
instances presented in Table 6.14. In fact the local search method does only improve 3.89%
of the total number of instance. It should be considered that for 269 out the 360 instances
there are not any shifting at all and these instances are not examined by the local search
method. This leaves 91 instances with initial shifting from the Main algorithm in Ding
and Chou (2015), for which the local search has run. By considering this fact the local

Table 6.14: Results for instances improved by LS algorithm
In Table 6.14 the results of the 14 improved instances are shown in a more detailed view
than previously. As it can be observed nine out of 14 improved instances have a long
transport matrix, while the remaining improved instance has a mix transport matrix. It
should further be noticed that none of the improved instances has N = 5, and only three
improved instances has N = 8, leaving 11 out 14 with N = 10. Regarding the capacity
there is not any clear pattern for when the LS algorithm performs best, since seven out of
eight different capacities are represented among the 14 improved instances. It should be
noticed that for half of the instances, the LS algorithm only locates one improving swap.
Instance 11 is the one, where the LS algorithm has been able to make most improving
swaps, where eight swaps are made obtaining an improvement 15 shifts.
The average shifting ratios presented in Ding and Chou (2015) shows the results for in-
stances varying from P = 300 to P = 10, 000 and N = 4 to N = 20. The implemented
Main algorithm from Ding and Chou (2015) has in average a good performance. The
performance of the average shifting ratios for either long- or mix transportation matrices
are worse than the results presented in Ding and Chou (2015), while the average shifting
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 6
63
ratios for short transportation matrices is better than the presented ones. It is however
worth to notice that the instances in Ding and Chou (2015) vary in tiers from T = 6 to
T = 10 and stacks from S = 50 to S = 1, 000, while the instances in the presented dataset
vary from T = 10 to T = 40 and stacks from S = 50 to S = 500. The use of much higher
stacks in the used benchmark complicates the loading procedure even more and it is ex-
pected to have a higher relative performance than the one form Ding and Chou (2015).
As it has been shown previously in this chapter, the LS algorithm do not improve the
neither the objective value or the relative performance for far most instances. Since the
LS algorithm only tries to optimize the stowage plan by swapping tagged containers,
is the corresponding neighbourhood for the local search method limited. Furthermore
is the local search limited to only accepting improving swaps of two containers, which
restrict the neighbourhood as well. This is further conﬁrmed by the average used com-
putational times for the Main- and LS algorithm, which are fairly the same and beneath
1 second of computational time for all instances with both algorithms.
6.3
Performance With Implemented Weight Constraint
The original implemented algorithm from Ding and Chou (2015) does not take account
for container weights and hereby neither the constraint from Section 5.4, which speciﬁes
a weight limit for each stack on the vessel. The extended algorithm is referred to as the
3-step algorithm in this thesis. The 3-step algorithm is a three step local search heuristic
carried out in order to obtain feasible stowage plans. This section does initially presents
results for the Main algorithm with the implemented stack weight constraint. Next av-
erage and worst case shifting for the 3-step algorithm are presented as well as average
shifting ratios.
6.3.1
Initial Performance With Weight Constraint
Table 6.15 shows the percentage of stacks exceeding the weight limit, ωs, where the re-
ported percentage is the lowest among ﬁve different instances. The reported percentage
of heavy stacks is calculated as the total number of stacks exceeding the weight limit in
the instance divided by N · S. Table 6.15 does in other words show how big a percentage
of all stacks for all ports, which is infeasible with respect to the stack weight constraint.
It can be observed in Table 6.15 that none of the solved instances with the Main algorithm
are feasible, if the weight constraint is considered. It is however expected that there
would exist stacks which exceeds the weight limit, since the Main algorithm does not
take this constraint into consideration. Even though none of the instances are feasible,
there are a tendency towards that short transportation matrices have most stacks above
the limit. It is an instance with a mix transport matrix, N = 5 and P = 15, 000, which is
closest to be a feasible solution with 9.68% stacks above the weight limit. Additionally
it should be noticed that the minimum percentage of stacks above the weight limit is as

6.3.2
Performance Of Algorithm With Implemented Weight Constraint
The incorporation of the stack weight constraints requires that the Main algorithm is also
extended. This extension consists of a three-step local search heuristic, 3-step algorithm,
which generates feasible stowage plans and is further described in Section 5.4.
Table 6.16 and Table 6.17 present the average and worst case shifting values obtained
with the 3-step algorithm for all instances in the dataset.
As one could expect the average shifting in Table 6.16 is higher than the objective val-
ues from the Main- and LS algorithm. First one can observe that even for instances with
N = 5, the average objective values are often very high. The average shifting for instances
with P = 4, 000 and a long transport matrix is 52.6 shifts, which is higher than the biggest
average shifting for both the Main- and LS algorithm (see Table 6.8). Next it is possible
to see that the average shifting is very high with N = 8 or N = 10 Furthermore does
the average objective value increases with the capacity and complexity of the instances
as well as it increases with N. There are two exceptions of this for long transportation
matrices for instances with P = 8, 000 and P = 15, 000, where the average shifting is
higher for instances with N = 8 than N = 10. Additionally it can be seen that the aver-
age shifting for instances with N = 5 is highest for long transportation matrices. When
considering instances with N = 8 or N = 10, the average shifting is highest for instances
with either a mix- or short transportation matrix. Finally it is worth to notice the very
high average objective value for the biggest instances with 806.4, 735.6 and 617.8 average
shifts for instances with respectively long-, mix and short transportation matrix, which
has very high average objective values compared to those obtained without considering
the stack weight constraint.
Table 6.17 presents the worst case instances in terms of shifting for the 3-step algorithm.

The average shifting ratios are presented in Table 6.18. Here it is clear to see that the
implementation of the stack weight constraint has a huge impact on the relative perfor-

age shifting ratio is for instances with mix transportation matrices and N = 10. It is
9.21%-points higher with the 3-step algorithm than the average shifting ratio with the LS
algorithm.

does obtain feasible solutions for all instances in the dataset but often with a bad solu-
