in Subsection 5.5.1. The construction of containers weights so close to the vessel capac-
ity in the most utilized port, heavily inﬂuence the performance of the 3-step algorithm.
The number of feasible swaps between containers decreases, since most stacks are either
loaded to their absolute maximum or very close to this limit. It is therefore not possible
to swap a heavier container onto a stack loaded to it’ maximum weight capacity.
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 7
Sensitivity Analysis
This chapter presents a sensitivity analysis for the implemented stack weight constraint.
As described in Subsection 5.5.1 the dataset is tight with respect to the predeﬁned weight
limit ωs = 20 · T and the given container weights for far most of the instances. The
sensitivity analysis will perform an analysis of how the results would be inﬂuenced by
different ωs. This evaluation is carried out for six different stack weight limits including
the original limit. The different ωs are given by enlarging the original limit by a percent-
age of the original limit, the chosen enlargements are 0.5%, 1%, 2%, 2.5% and 5%. The
new results will be compare to the three implemented algorithms; Main-, LS and 3-step
presented in Chapter 5.
All experiments are conducted on an Intel (R) Xeon (R) with a 2.30 Ghz processor and 16
GB RAM with the dataset presented in Subsection 5.5.1.
The chapter will be divided into three sections, one for each type of transportation matrix.
The tables presented in Section 7.1 to Section 7.3 are divide into different tables depend-
ing on the the number of ports, N. Each table presents the average number of shifts for
instances matching the transportation matrix and N. In the tables each row represent
a different solution approach, where the ones presented below the 3-step algorithm are
with a enlargement of ωs. Furthermore does each column in the tables represent differ-
ent values of P. Given this set-up does each number in the presented tables represent an
average of ﬁve different instances.
7.1
Evaluation Of Long Transportation Matrices
This section provides a sensitivity analysis for the average shifting results of instances
with a long transportation matrix in Table 7.1 to Table 7.3.
Table 7.1 presents the average shifting of the three algorithms; Main-, LS- and 3-step
algorithm when N = 5. One can observe that the implementation of the stack weight
limit constraint causes a huge number of extra shifts. These extra shifts are heavily in-
67
==PAGE:84==
68
SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS
ﬂuenced by the tight dataset, since average shifting results similar to the Main- and LS
algorithm can be obtained by enlarging the limit by 0.5%.
When examine the inﬂuence of different stack weight limits on the average shifting for
slightly longer routes of N = 8 with a long transportation matrix. It is possible to see in
Table 7.2 that by increasing ωs by 1% the average shifting values are similar to the ones
obtained with the Main algorithm. An enlargement of 5% would give average results
similar to those obtained with the LS algorithm. It is only the average shifting for in-
stances with P = 3, 000, which is higher than the LS algorithm with an enlargement of
5%. Even though huge number of shifts where needed with the initial stack weight limit.
The average shifting of instances with N = 10 is shown in Table 7.3. The tight alloca-
tion of container weights in relation to the original ωs causes average shifting for the
3-step algorithm to be very high. Like instances with N = 8, it is only required to enlarge
the limit by 1% in order to obtain similar results as the Main algorithm with an exception
of instances with P = 1, 500. Those instances do require an enlargement of the limit by
5%, if average shifting values similar to the Main algorithm are wanted. Additionally it
should be observed that the results are actually improved with an enlargement of limit
by 1% for instances with P = 500 and P = 3, 000, compared to the average of the Main-
and LS algorithm. Those improvements are caused by using a relocation move as a the
new neighbourhood operator.

7.2
Evaluation Of Mix Transportation Matrices
Table 7.4 to Table 7.6 present a comparison between the three implemented algorithms
and the enlargement of ωs considering all instances with a mix transportation matrix.
Table 7.4 presents the average shifting for instances with N = 5. As it can be observed,
even the 3-step algorithm manages to solve all of these instances to optimality. It is there-
fore not necessary to enlarge ωs at all in order to obtain similar average shifting values as
with the two other algorithms.
Table 7.5 presents the average objectives values for instances of N = 8. Again the 3-
step algorithm obtains some very high average objective values. By increasing ωs with
0.5%, the use of extra shifts in average can almost be eliminated compared to the Main
algorithm. An enlargement of ωs by 1% gives completely the same average shifting as
the Main algorithm.
How different ωs inﬂuence the average shifting are presented in Table 7.6 when N = 10.
With the original ωs, the average solution quality of the 3-step algorithm is very bad com-
pared to the other implemented algorithms. It is only needed to increase ωs by 0.5% to
obtain fairly good results, close to the ones obtained with the Main algorithm. In order
to obtain completely similar results as with the Main algorithm, it is necessary to enlarge
==PAGE:86==
70
SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS
the limit by 5%. Almost smilar results can be obtained if ωs is increased by 2.5%. It is
only the instances with P = 3, 000, where the average shifting is higher than the objective
value of the Main algorithm.

7.3
Evaluation Of Short Transportation Matrices
The evaluation of how different ωs inﬂuence’ the average shifting for instances with a
short transportation matrix is presented in Table 7.7 to Table 7.9.
Table 7.7 presents the average shifting for the three algorithms as well as the different
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 7
71
stack weight limits for the 3-step algorithm for instances with N = 5. The Main- and
LS algorithm solves all these instances to optimality. The implementation of the stack
weight limit slightly increases the average shifting for instances with P ≤8, 000. In or-
der to obtain only optimal results including the stack weight constraint, it is necessary
to enlarge ωs by 2% of the original value. It should be noticed that the 3-step algorithm
without any enlargement of ωs obtains fairly good results. With an increase of only 0.5%
of the stack weight limit, three instances would require only a single shift, while only a
single instance requires one shift with an increased by 1%.
Table 7.8 shows the average shifting for all instances with N = 8. The Main- and LS
algorithm present the same average shifting, almost only consisting of optimal solutions.
The results for the 3-step algorithm are very huge compared to the other two algorithms.
But with an increase of ωs by only 0.5%, the average number of shifts are signiﬁcantly
improved. With this enlargement the performance of the 3-step algorithm is fairly close
to optimal and way better than the ones with the original ωs. By enlarging ωs to a 2%
increase in total, the 3-step algorithm obtains the same average shifting as both the Main-
and LS algorithm.
Table 7.9 presents the average shifting for instances with N = 10 and a short transporta-
tion matrix. Even though the Main- and LS algorithm solves almost all of these instances
to optimality, the 3-step algorithm does presents very high average objective values. In
order to obtain the same results as with the other two algorithms, it is necessary to enlarge
ωs by 5%, while an increase of 1% gives results very close to these.

7.4
Evaluation Of Computational Times For All Matrix Types
Another performance measurement is the computational time measured in milliseconds
(ms.) for the three algorithms. This section presents an evaluation of the computational
times for the three implemented algorithms as well as the computational times for the
different ωs introduced in the previous section. Table 7.10 to Table 7.12 are slightly dif-
ferent than the above presented tables. The tables are still divided into different tables
depending on N, where each row refers to an implemented algorithm and each column
to instances with the same P. The tables considering the computational times do not take
different transportation matrices into account, which mean that each cell is the average
computational time of 15 different instances.
In Table 7.10 the average computational time used by the Main- and LS algorithm is
are very similar for instances with N = 5. Further it can be observed that the used av-
erage computational time increases a lot, when the weight constraint is more tight. As
described in Section 7.1 to Section 7.3, the 3-step algorithm does obtain similar results as
the other two algorithms if ωs is increased by 1% or more. This is reﬂected in the com-
putational times, where the 3-step algorithm uses fairly the same computational time as
the other algorithms for these enlargements. The computational times are signiﬁcantly
higher for the 3-step algorithm with the original ωs, due to a limited number of feasible
solutions when ωs is smaller. The maximum computational time used for a single in-
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 7
73
stance is 180,123 ms. (3 minutes), which is an instance with a short transportation matrix,
N = 5 and P = 20, 000, see Appendix H. The maximum computational time is far from
the allowed time limit of 10 minutes, this is due to design of the termination criterion
based on computational time, described in Section 5.4.
Table 7.11 shows the average computational time for all instances with N = 8. As in
Table 7.10 the average computational time used is highly correlated with average shift-
ing. If the average shifting for the 3-step algorithm is close to the one for the Main- or LS
algorithm, the average computational time used is also similar. Due to this, the used com-
putational time for the 3-step algorithm is in the same level as the other two algorithms,
when ωs increases with 1% or more. The maximum running time for a single instance
with N = 8 is 158,957 ms. (2.65 minutes), this instance has a short transportation matrix
and P = 20, 000, see Appendix H.
The computational times for instances with the longest route and N = 10 is presented in
Table 7.12. It should be noticed that the computational times for these instances in aver-
age are higher than the presented computational times in Table 7.10 and Table 7.11. As
with the instances with N = 5 and N = 8, the computational time used by the 3-step al-
gorithm is in the same level as the other two algorithms, when ωs is enlarged by at least
1%. The average computational time used are signiﬁcantly higher for the 3-step algo-
rithm with the original ωs. Instances with a capacity of 20,000 requires in average 99,152
ms. (1.65 minutes) compared to a maximum of 72,807 ms. (1.21 minutes) and 60,176 ms.
(1 minute) for respectively instances with N = 8 and N = 5. The maximum running
time for a single instance with N = 10 is also the instance with the biggest overall com-
putational time. This instances has a short transportation matrix with P = 20, 000 and a
running time of 203,324 ms. (3.39 minutes).

This chapter shows that the performance of the 3-step algorithm is many times worse
than the Main- and LS algorithm both in terms of average objective values and average

computational times. This is however expected, since the formulation of the problem has
been extended to include the stack weight constraint.
The sensitivity analysis does show that the performance of the 3-step algorithm is heav-
ily inﬂuence by the stack weight limit as one could expect. The problem formulation for
both the Main- and LS algorithm can be seen as including the stack weight constraint, just
with an inﬁnitive stack weight limit. The sensitivity analysis does however show that the
3-step algorithm is applicable of obtaining fairly good solutions, with an enlarged stack
weight limit of as little as 0.5% of the predeﬁned weight limit. Furthermore it is shown
in the sensitivity analysis that a stack weight limit increase of 2% gives similar results as
the other implemented algorithms for both average objective values and average compu-
tational times.
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 8
Conclusion
The aim of this thesis has been to investigate the possibility of obtaining better solutions
to the simple placement heuristic, developed by Ding and Chou (2015). The solution
developed in this thesis was then compared to the performance of the modiﬁed simple
placement heuristic to the mathematical model by Avriel et al. (1998). Furthermore, the
aim of the thesis has been to investigate the option of obtaining good feasible solutions
with the placement heuristic while considering a stack weight constraint. The solution
approach exchanges the original stowage plan in the placement heuristic with a local
search containing a swap operator to minimize the number of shifts. The solution ap-
proach for including the stack weight limit constraint is to obtain an initial stowage plan
with the placement heuristic and then use a 3-step heuristic to obtain a good feasible
stowage plan within a time limit of 10 computational minutes.
The computational results shows that the mathematical model is not applicable of ﬁnd-
ing feasible solutions within 10 minutes of computational time,whenever either the num-
ber of ports or the capacity increases yet does the mathematical model solve 43% of the
dataset and 39% to optimality. The placement algorithm from Ding and Chou (2015)
manages to obtain good feasible solutions for all 360 instances within 1 second of com-
putational time. The extension of the placement heuristic with a local search with a
swap neighbourhood ﬁnds better solutions for 15% of all initial stowage plans with over-
stowage, without any signiﬁcant computational time increase. Furthermore, the com-
putational results show that the 3-step algorithm is able to ﬁnd feasible solutions for all
instances in the dataset within 10 minutes. The performance of the 3-step algorithm is
not very functional for the existing dataset, neither in terms of computational time or
objective value. The sensitivity analysis concludes that the tight relationship of container
weights and the stack weight limit is the reason for the unsuccessful performance of the
3-step algorithm. This can be explained by the fact, that in 77% of the instances, more
than 99% of the total vessel weight capacity was used in at least one port. The sensitivity
analysis shows that a the stack weight limit enlargement of 0.5% is enough to secure a
fairly good performance of the 3-step algorithm, while an enlargement of 2% results in
75
==PAGE:92==
76
CONCLUSION
almost the same performance as the original placement heuristic, without using consid-
erable increased computational time.
Although the average solution quality of the placement heuristic is improved by the lo-
cal search, the improvements are often small and not considerable relative to the number
of loaded containers. Thus, it is urgent that more time is spent to further develop this
extension. In conclusive terms, the performance of the 3-step algorithm is as equal to
the original placement heuristic in the speciﬁc case where there is only a slight difference
between the total container weight and the vessel weight capacity.
8.1
Further Work
The limited improvements for the extension to the placement heuristic motivates further
research to be done in order to be able to improve a higher percentage of solutions with
initial overstowage from the placement heuristic. A solution approach could be devel-
oped by expanding the neighbourhood with different operators considering more than
two containers at a time. Another alternative solution approach could be to investigate
the possibility of implementing the use of meta heuristics or to include a forecast of the
loading list for the subsequent port. The idea of a look-ahead procedure is basically the
same idea as with voluntary shifts, where voluntary shifts can be made based on the
loading list forecast, in order to prevent more expensive shifts in the subsequent ports.
As the 3-step algorithm was able to adjust the initial stowage plans to good feasible
solutions with respect to the stack weight constraint, one could be motivated to use
this approach evaluate the inﬂuence of more complex stowage planning problems in
future research. A more complex stowage planning problem would be to add one or sev-
eral other constraints to the existing formulation, such as additional weight constraints
(where heavier containers cannot be stowed on top of lighter ones) or considerations of
balance constraints, dealing with the vessel stability. Additionally, the inﬂuence of mixed
constraints, considering different size and types of containers could also be considered in
the future implementation.
Finally, the solutions for all three algorithms were obtained without using half of the
computational time limit. This outlines a potential for future research to either increase
the neighbourhood or the problem complexity. Furthermore, this permits future research
to have a better termination criterion for the algorithm, where additional computational
time can be used for those cases where it is needed.

Appendix A
Placement Heuristic From Ding and
Chou (2015)
Below is the placement heuristic presented in Ding and Chou (2015) presented. The
placement heuristic is furthermore the placement heuristic known as the Main algorithm
in this thesis.
Input. S0, T, N;
Step 1. Let i = 1;
Step 2. Let Si = Si−1 and wi =

wij

where wij = Tij, j ∈{i + 1, ..., N};
Step 3. Unload all i-containers and those blocking them;
Step 4. Load containers on board in a descending order of their destination as follows:
Step 4.1. Let j = N;
Step 4.2. Follow the undermentioned rules sequentially whenever applicable;
Rule 1. If wij = 0. Let j = j −1 and go to Step 4.3;
Rule 2. If there exists only one empty stack in the bay. Load all unassigned con-
tainers into that stack in order. Let j = i and go to Step 4.3;
Rule 3. If there exists only one eligible stack in the bay. Apply the MVSD pro-
cedure to determine the G (m), the number of containers to be unloaded.
Unload G (m) containers from the stack, then load all unassigned contain-
ers into that stack in order. Let j = i and go to Step 4.3
Rule 4. If j = N, and the set of empty stacks and eligible N-Top in order stacks,
denoted by E (4), is not empty. Load one N-container into the stack with
the most number of empty slots in set E (4) repeatedly until no such load-
ing movement can be carried out. If wij = 0. Let j = j −1 and go to Step
4.3;

PLACEMENT HEURISTIC FROM DING AND CHOU (2015)
Rule 5. If j < N, and the set of eligible j-top in order stacks, denoted by E (5), is
not empty. Load as many j-container as possible into stacks in set E (5)
based on the following priority sequence: with the least number of empty
slots, with the furthest destination of 2nd layer containers, with the most
number of 1st layer containers, ..., (repeating the last two items by increas-
ing the index by 1 at each time), until no such loading movements can be
carried out. If wij = 0. Let j = j −1 and go to Step 4.3;
Rule 6. If j < N, and the set of empty stacks, denoted by E (6), is not empty. Load
as many j-containers as possible into the stack with the most number of
empty slots in set E (6) until no such loading movements can be carried
out. If wij = 0. Let j = j −1 and go to Step 4.3;
Rule 7. If j < N, and the set of all eligible l-min (l > j) in order stacks, denoted
by E (7), is not empty. Load as many j-container as possible into stacks
in set E (7) based on the following priority sequence: with the furthest
destination of 1st layer containers, with the most number of empty slots,
with the furthest destination of 2nd layer containers, with the most number
of 1st layer containers, ..., (repeating the last two items by increasing the
index by 1 at each time), until no such loading movements can be carried
out. If wij = 0. Let j = j −1 and go to Step 4.3;
Rule 8. If j < N, and the set of all eligible l-min (l ≥j) out of order stacks, denoted
by E (8), is not empty. Load as many j-container as possible into stacks
in set E (8) based on the following priority sequence: with the furthest
destination of 1st layer containers, with the most number of empty slots,
with the furthest destination of 2nd layer containers, with the most number
of 1st layer containers, ..., (repeating the last two items by increasing the
index by 1 at each time), until no such loading movements can be carried
out. All marked containers in those ﬁlled stacks will be unmarked. If wij =
0. Let j = j −1 and go to Step 4.3;
Rule 9.
Rule 10. If j = i + 1, load all unassigned j-containers into the remaining empty

go to Step 4.3;
Step 4.3. If j ≥i + 1, go to Step 4.2;
Step 5. Record Si, which is the stowage plan upon leaving port i;
Step 6. If i < N −1, let i = i + 1 and go to Step 2;
Output. Si, i ∈{1, ..., N −1}.

Appendix B
Mathematical Model From Ding and
Chou (2015)
The mathematical formulation presented in Ding and Chou (2015), the mathematical
model used in this thesis is the one from Avriel et al. (1998). The two mathematical
models are however very similar and has the same overall performance.
In the proposed model S is the number of stacks in the bay and T the number of tiers
in a stack. Let N be the number of ports visited by the vessel sequentially. The trans-
portation matrix τ = [Tij] is a (N · N matrix, where [Tij] (i, j ∈1, ..., N) is the number of
containers with origin in port i and destination in port j. Tij is an upper triangular matrix,
so Tij ≥0∀i, j and Tij = 0∀i ≥j. A transportation matrix is considered feasible if ?? is
satisﬁed, where P = S × T is the capacity of the vessel.

xijs(t+1)
∀i ∈{1, ..., N −1}, ∀r ∈{1, ..., R −1},
∀c ∈{1, ..., C}
(B.7)
N
∑
j=i+1
yijst ≤
N
∑
j=i+1
yijs(t+1)
∀i ∈{1, ..., N −1}, ∀t ∈{1, ..., T −1},
∀s ∈{1, ..., S}
(B.8)
yijst ≤xijst
∀i ∈{2, ..., N −1}, ∀j ∈{i + 1, ..., N},
∀t ∈{1, ..., T}, ∀s ∈{1, ..., S}
(B.9)
y(i+1)jst ≤xijst
∀i ∈{2, ..., N −2}, ∀j ∈{i + 2, ..., N},
∀t ∈{1, ..., T}, ∀s ∈{1, ..., S}
(B.10)
y1jst = 0
∀j ∈{2, ..., N}, ∀t ∈{1, ..., T −1},
∀s ∈{1, ..., S}
(B.11)
xijst ∈{0, 1}
∀i ∈{1, ..., N −1}, ∀j ∈{i + 1, ..., N},
∀t ∈{1, ..., T}, ∀s ∈{1, ..., S}
(B.12)
yijst ∈{0, 1}
∀i ∈{1, ..., N −1}, ∀j ∈{i + 1, ..., N},
∀t ∈{1, ..., T}, ∀s ∈{1, ..., S}
(B.13)
The objective function (B.2) of the binary LP formulation is to minimize the number of
shifts as the difference between loading and unloading movements in all ports except the
ﬁrst one.
Constraint (B.3) states that the all containers with origin in port 1 is loaded onto the ves-
sel, while Constraint (B.4) ensures that containers loaded in the other ports with destined
to a subsequent port are loaded at the vessel. Constraint (B.5) and (B.6) secures that there
at most is one container occupies a slot (s, t) on the vessel by either being loaded or al-
ready being stowed in the slot. Constraint (B.7) and (B.8) assures that it is not allowed to
==PAGE:101==
B
85
have a container stowed or loaded on top of an empty slot, which here by secures that
all containers have support from below. Finally Constraint (B.9) and (B.10) make sure
that containers are actually unloaded and then loaded again if it is necessary to perform
a shift. This is hereby also the connection between the two decision variables xijst and
yijst.
==PAGE:102==
86
MATHEMATICAL MODEL FROM DING AND CHOU (2015)
==PAGE:103==
Appendix C
Java Code Description
This appendix is a guideline for the implemented java code used for the computational
results in Chapter 6 and the sensitivity analysis in Chapter 7.
The class MyOwnAlgorithm executes the 10 rules from the placement heuristic proposed
in Ding and Chou (2015). Within this class it is possible for the user to deﬁne, which of
the implemented algorithms that should be executed. The public static boolean runSwap
turns on the LS algorithm, when runSwap = true and the Main algorithm otherwise. Fur-
thermore must the public static double stackLimitMultiplier take a high value (e.g. 1,000)
in order to run the algorithms without considering the stack weight constraint. When
stackLimitMultiplier = 1, then the predeﬁned stack weight limit is implemented for each
stack. Considering the sensitivity analysis in Chapter 7 does the stackLimitMultiplier
takes the values 1.005 (0.5%), 1.01 (1%), 1.02 (2%),1.025 (2.5%) and 1.05 (5%). Finally the
3-step algorithm is turned on when the public static boolean runFeasible = true and turned
off otherwise.
The class Main executes the program, here it is possible for the user to either run the
total benchmark by having all = true or a single instance if single = true. If single = true
the path of the exact instance has to be deﬁned in the String singleInstance
87
